Acknowledgment of a Bequest After Division: Istihsan vs Qiyas
Ruling and proof for bequest acknowledgment after division

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on acknowledgment of a bequest after division and distinguish it from the qiyas position of Zufar.

Core Idea
Acknowledging a bequest as a common share limits liability to one-third of what you possess, not half.

When two heirs divide an estate, and later one acknowledges a prior bequest from the deceased, how much must he pay? Qiyas (analogy) suggests half his share, but the Hanafi school, by istihsan, rules for one-third. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this preference and the key distinction between common shares and specific debts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the core ruling
State: after division of the estate, one son acknowledges that his father had left a bequest of one-third. The Hanafi ruling (عندنا) is that the acknowledging son pays one-third of his own share.
This is not half or the full one-third of the total estate.
2
Examine the qiyas (Zufar's position)
Zufar argues by qiyas: since the acknowledgment implies three equal shares (legatee, son A, son B), and son B denies or is absent, son A should pay half of what he holds to the legatee – because the absent son’s share is treated as nonexistent, so the present property is divided equally.
This is the apparent logical outcome if one treats the acknowledgment as defining specific shares.
3
Understand the basis of istihsan
Hanafi istihsan: the acknowledgment is of a common one-third (ثلث شائع) in the whole estate, not a specific portion. Since the estate is owned jointly by both sons, the acknowledging son only controls half. Therefore, he is acknowledging one-third of what he possesses, i.e., one-third of his share.
The key phrase is 'ثلث شائع' – a common share, not a determined item.
4
Contrast with acknowledgment of a debt
If one son acknowledges a debt owed to a third party, he pays the full debt from his share. This is because a debt is a liability against the estate, not a fraction of ownership. The distinction confirms that the bequest ruling is specific to shares in the property.
The text cites 'غاية البيان' for the detailed distinction.
5
Summarize the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The difference in rulings: Hanafi (mufta bihi) = pay 1/3 of your share; Zufar’s qiyas = pay 1/2 of your share. The Hanafi position is more favorable to the acknowledging heir.
This shows how istihsan can lead to a different outcome from strict analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أقر أحد الابنين بعد القسمة بوصيّة أبيه بالثلث دَفَعَ ثُلُثَ نصيبه، هذا عندنا، والقياس أن يعطيه نصف ما في يده، وهو قولُ زُفَرَه ؛ لأن إقرارَهُ بالثلث يوجب مساواته إياه، وجه الاستحسان : أنه أقرّ بثلث شائع، فيكون مقراً بثلث ما في يده. توضيحه: اقتسم الابنان تركة الأب ثم أقر أحدهما لرجل أنّ أباه أوصى له بثلث ماله دفع المقرّ ثلث نصيبه له. قوله : لأن إقراره ... الخ ؛ يعني أن في زعم المقر أن المال يقسم أثلاثاً ونصيب الموصى له ونصيبُ كلّ واحدٍ من الابنين سواء ، فلَمَّا أنكر أحدهما أو غابَ جُعِلَ كأنّ نصيبه لم يكن ، فيقسم الباقي عليهما نصفين ؛ لأنَّ نصيبهما سواء. كذا «غاية البيان». قوله : ووجه الاستحسان : أنه أقرّ بثلث شائع ؛ في التركة التي نصفها في يدِ المقر ونصفها في يد غيره، فيكون مقراً بثلث ما في يده، بخلاف ما إذا أقر أحد الابنين بدين لغيره، فإنه يقضي للمقر له بكل الدين من نصيب المقر، ووجه الفرق في «غاية البيان».
If one of the two sons, after division, acknowledges the bequest of his father of one-third, he pays one-third of his share. This is our view. The analogy (qiyas) would require that he give him half of what is in his hand, which is the saying of Zufar, because his acknowledgment of one-third would necessitate equality with him. The basis of istihsan is: he acknowledged one-third as a common share (sha'i'), so he is acknowledging one-third of what is in his hand. Explanation: The two sons divided the father's estate, then one of them acknowledged to a man that his father had bequeathed to him one-third of his wealth, so the acknowledging son pays one-third of his share to him. He said: because his acknowledgment... means that according to the acknowledgment of the acknowledgee, the wealth is to be divided into three parts, and the share of the legatee and each of the two sons are equal. But when one of them denies or is absent, it is as if his share did not exist, so the remainder is divided between them in half, because their shares are equal. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. He said: The basis of istihsan is that he acknowledged a common one-third in the estate, half of which is in the hand of the acknowledgee and half in the hand of another, so he is acknowledging one-third of what is in his hand. This is unlike when one of the two sons acknowledges a debt due to another, then the debtor is judged to pay the entire debt from the share of the acknowledgee. The basis of the difference is in Ghayat al-Bayan.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling and its proof regarding the scenario where one of two sons, after having divided their father's inheritance, acknowledges a bequest of one-third to a third party. According to the Hanafi madhhab (عندنا), the acknowledging son pays only one-third of his share (i.e., one-sixth of the total estate?). The qiyas (analogy), as held by Zufar, would require him to pay half of his share, because his acknowledgment of one-third would suggest that he, the legatee, and the other son are equal, so the acknowledged share should be divided equally between the legatee and the acknowledgee? Actually Zufar's qiyas: since he claims the entire estate should be divided into three shares, but one of the sons (the non-acknowledging) denies or is absent, so the present share (the acknowledgee's share) should be split half and half? The text explains the Hanafi istihsan: the acknowledgment is of a common one-third (ثلث شائع) in the entire estate, which consists of two halves: one in the hand of the acknowledgee and one in the hand of the other son. Therefore, the acknowledgee is only acknowledging one-third of what he himself possesses, i.e., one-third of his own share. The commentary contrasts this with a debt: if one son acknowledges a debt payable to another, he must pay the full debt from his own share, because debt is a liability that attaches to the estate, not a specified share.

Key Terms
الإستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in favor of a more equitable or practical ruling.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning: applying the ruling of an original case to a new case due to shared effective cause.
ثلث شائع (thuluth sha'i'): A common one-third share: an undivided fraction of the whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi text, what is the distinction that leads to the istihsan ruling for a bequest as opposed to a debt?
A) A bequest is a common share; a debt is a specific liability.
B) A bequest is from the father; a debt is from the son.
C) A bequest is after division; a debt is before division.
D) A bequest is for a third; a debt is for a fixed amount.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, acknowledging a bequest as a common share (thuluth sha'i') results in paying one-third of one's own share, not half, based on istihsan.

Ruling on the Offspring of a Bequeathed Slave-Girl Born After the Testator's Death
Ruling on the offspring of a bequeathed slave-girl born after the testator's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether the offspring of a bequeathed slave-girl born after the testator's death is included in the bequest and explain the proof based on the name (increase) principle.

Core Idea
A child born to a bequeathed slave-girl after the testator's death is included in the bequest as an increase (nama') belonging to the estate.

This lesson examines a specific issue in the law of bequest (wasiyyah): if a testator bequeaths a slave-girl and she gives birth after his death, does the legatee also receive the child? The Hanafi position, as taught by Abu Hanifa, is that the child is included as a dependent following the mother, provided the total value does not exceed one-third of the estate. The reasoning is based on the principle that any increase occurring before division is treated as part of the deceased's ownership.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and basic ruling
Identify the key facts: a testator bequeaths a slave-girl, and after his death she gives birth. The ruling: both mother and child belong to the legatee, provided they come out of the one-third.
The child is treated as an increase (nama') that follows the mother.
2
Determine the condition and its consequence
If the combined value of mother and child exceeds one-third of the estate, the legatee takes only a proportionate share up to one-third from the combined value.
The legatee does not automatically take the excess; it reverts to the heirs.
3
Understand the legal rationale (dalil)
The proof is based on qiyas: any increase (nama') occurring before division is deemed to occur under the deceased's ownership. This is evidenced by the fact that such increases are used to pay debts and execute bequests.
The child is considered an accretion to the bequeathed property.
4
Note the distinction regarding permission
If the heirs permit the bequest beyond one-third, they are doing a gratuitous act and may still refuse delivery. This contrasts with a straightforward bequest exceeding one-third where the heirs' permission makes it binding.
The legatee's right is contingent on actual delivery; he cannot compel if the heirs change their minds.
5
Identify the authority
This ruling is attributed to Abu Hanifa and is recorded in Ghayat al-Bayan. It is part of the zahir al-riwayah corpus.
Other schools may have differing views on the inclusion of after-born offspring.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن وَلَدَتِ الموصى بها بعد موته فهما له، إن خرجا من الثلث وإلا أخذ الثلث منها، ثم منه أي بعد الإجازة، فإنه إن أجاز، فإجازته تبرع، فله أن يمتنع من التسليم، هذا عند أبي حنيفة. (فإن ولدت الموصى بها بعد موته فهما له): أي الأمة الموصى بها وولدها، إن خرجا من الثلث وإلا أخذ الثلث منها، ثم منه، هذا عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن التبع بمال الغير، فيتوقف على إجازته، وإذا أجاز يكون تبرعاً منه، فله أن يمنع من التسليم، بخلاف ما إذا أوصى بالزيادة على الثلث، وأجازت الورثة؛ لأن الوصية في مخرجها صحيحة بمصادقتها ملك نفسه، والامتناع لحق الورثة، فإذا أجازَ سقط حقهم فتنفذ من جهة الموصي. قوله: فهما له؛ أي الأمة والولد للموصى له، إنما كان الولد والأمة للموصى له إذا خرجا من الثلث؛ لأن ما يحدث من النماء قبل القسمة يحدث على حكم ملك الميت، بدليل أنه يقضي به ديونه، وينفذ وصاياه، فينفذ الوصية بالثلث فيهما جميعاً؛ في الأم أصالة، وفي الولد تبعاً؛ لاتصاله بالأم. كذا في «غاية البيان».
If the bequeathed slave-girl gives birth after his death, then both belong to him (the legatee), provided they come out of the one-third; otherwise, he takes one-third from her, then from him (the offspring) after permission. Because if he permits, his permission is a gratuitous act, so he may refuse delivery. This according to Abu Hanifa. (If the bequeathed slave-girl gives birth after his death, then both belong to him): i.e., the bequeathed slave-girl and her child, if they come out of the one-third; otherwise, he takes one-third from her, then from him (the offspring). This according to Abu Hanifa, because the child is connected to property belonging to another, so it is suspended upon his permission. And when he permits, it is a gratuitous act from him, so he may prevent delivery, unlike if he bequeaths more than the one-third and the heirs permit, because the bequest in its origin is valid as it encounters his own property, and the refusal is due to the right of the heirs. So when they permit, their right falls, and it is executed from the testator's side. He said: 'both belong to him'; i.e., the slave-girl and the child belong to the legatee, only if they come out of the one-third. Because whatever increase occurs before division occurs under the ruling of the deceased's ownership, as evidenced by the fact that it is used to pay his debts and execute his bequests. So the bequest of the one-third applies to both: regarding the mother by origin, and regarding the child as a consequence, due to its connection to the mother. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan.
Source pages p. 609

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on the offspring of a bequeathed slave-girl born after the testator's death. According to Abu Hanifa, if the testator bequeaths a slave-girl, and she gives birth before the division of the estate, both the mother and the child go to the legatee, provided the total value of both does not exceed one-third of the estate. If they exceed the one-third, the legatee takes only one-third of the combined value from the mother and child together. The child is considered a part of the mother's value (follows her) because it was born after death and is an increase occurring under the deceased's ownership. The ruling is that the bequest applies to the mother primarily, and the child follows as a dependent (tabi'). The passage also explains that the legatee's right is subject to the heirs' permission if the bequest exceeds one-third; but even if they permit, they may later refuse delivery because it is a gratuitous act. This is contrasted with a bequest exceeding one-third that the heirs permit: in that case, the bequest is valid and binding because it originated from the testator's own property.

Key Terms
النماء (al-nama'): Increase or accretion: offspring or profit generated by property after the testator's death.
التبع (al-tabi'): That which follows: the child is considered a dependent of the mother in bequest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, if a bequeathed slave-girl and her child exceed one-third of the estate, the legatee takes:
A) Nothing
B) Only the mother
C) Only the child
D) A proportionate share up to one-third of the combined value

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the offspring of a bequeathed slave-girl born after the testator's death is included in the bequest as an increase (nama'), provided the total value does not exceed one-third of the estate.

Distribution of a Bequest: Mother Slave-Girl and Newborn Child (Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
Intra-Hanafi dispute over distribution of a bequest involving a slave-girl and her newborn child.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's rulings on the distribution of a bequest involving a slave-girl and her newborn, and apply the principle of asl and taba' to similar cases.

Core Idea
The mother is the original (asl); the child is an appendage (taba')—the appendage does not compete with the original in the one-third limit.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a bequest of a slave-girl who gives birth after the testator's death but before estate division. Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) differ on how to allocate the one-third bequest limit between the mother and child. The disagreement turns on whether the child is considered an appendage that cannot compete with the mother, or whether it should be treated equally as having entered the bequest. Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi principles of asl and taba' in bequests and the use of qiyas (analogical reasoning).

Guided Path
1
State the case and identify the one-third limit
The testator leaves 600 dirhams, a slave-girl worth 300, and she gives birth to a child worth 300 before division. Total estate: 1200 dirhams. One-third = 400. Determine how the legatee receives exactly 400 in value.
The numbers ensure the total bequest cannot exceed one-third of the estate.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's view and his reasoning
Abu Hanifa gives the legatee the entire mother (300) and one-third of the child (100). His reasoning: the mother is the original (asl) and the child is an appendage (taba'); the appendage does not compete with the original in the one-third limit.
Also he distinguishes bequest from sale: in sale, including the child does not annul part of the mother's transaction, but in bequest it would because the one-third limit is binding.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's view and their reasoning
The Sahibayn give the legatee two-thirds of each (200 from mother, 200 from child). Their reasoning: the child entered the bequest as an appendage while attached, so separation does not remove it; this is analogous to sale and manumission where an appendage is included.
They emphasize that the child was part of the bequest from the start.
4
Compare the proofs and the distinction
Abu Hanifa's objection: in sale, executing the sale in both does not annul any part of the original; in bequest, treating both equally would annul part of the mother's bequest since the total would exceed one-third. The Sahibayn maintain the analogy.
The disagreement is about the applicability of the qiyas to the bequest context.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, the legatee owns the mother fully and only one-third of the child. Under the Sahibayn, the legatee owns two-thirds of each. The total value received is the same (400), but the ownership shares differ, affecting subsequent transactions (e.g., sale, manumission).
This shows that the ikhtilaf is not merely theoretical but has real implications.

Source Text

He does not compete with the original, and according to them he takes from each according to the share. So if he has 600 dirhams and a slave-girl worth 300, and she gives birth to a child worth 300 dirhams after the death of the legatee until his wealth becomes 1200, then one-third of the wealth is 400. According to Abu Hanifa, the legatee gets the mother and one-third of the child, and according to them, two-thirds of each. Their argument: we have mentioned that the child entered the bequest as an appendage while attached, so it does not leave by separation, as in sale and manumission; thus the bequest is executed in both equally without prioritizing the mother. His argument: the mother is the original and the child is an appendage, and the appendage does not compete with the original. If we executed the bequest in both entirely, the bequest in part of the original would be annulled, and that is not permissible, unlike sale; because executing the sale does not lead to its annulment in the original. Rather, it remains complete and valid in it, except that part of the price is not offset against it due to it being offset against the child, invalid. When possession is connected, but the price is an appendage in sale, so the sale is concluded without mentioning it. He said: He only said 'she gave birth before division' because if she gave birth after division, then it belongs to the legatee; because it is pure growth of his property, due to his ownership being established in it after division.
Source pages p. 610

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the distribution of a bequest when a bequeathed slave-girl gives birth to a child before division of the estate. The case is from the Jami' al-Saghir of Imam Muhammad. Abu Hanifa holds that the mother (asl) is the primary object of the bequest, and the child (taba') is secondary, so the child cannot compete with the mother in taking from the one-third limit. Thus, the legatee takes the entire mother and only one-third of the child (since the child's value is 300, one-third is 100, totaling 400). The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that the child entered the bequest as an appendage while still attached to the mother, and separation does not remove it; thus both are treated equally, so the legatee takes two-thirds of each (200 from mother, 200 from child, also totaling 400). The difference is practical: under Abu Hanifa, the legatee gets full ownership of the mother, while under the Sahibayn, the legatee gets only two-thirds of the mother and two-thirds of the child. The author (the commentator) explains the reasoning: the Sahibayn analogize to sale and manumission where an appendage is included; Abu Hanifa distinguishes because in bequest, including the child would annul part of the mother's bequest, which is impermissible. The text also notes that if the child was born after division, it belongs solely to the legatee as growth of his property.

Key Terms
أصل (asl): The primary object of a bequest or transaction, here the mother slave-girl.
تبع (taba‘): An appendage or secondary item that follows the primary object, here the child born after the bequest.
مزاحمة (muzāḥamah): Competition for shares in a limited pool, here the one-third bequest limit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The dispute illustrates the Hanafi principle that an appendage (taba') does not compete with the original (asl) in the one-third bequest limit, and the use of qiyas with distinctions based on the nature of the transaction.

Bequest to Free a Slave with Partial Loss of Funds
Intra-Hanafi dispute on partial depletion of bequest funds for freeing a slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding partial depletion of bequest funds for freeing a slave and identify the underlying principle (right of Allah vs right of slave) that determines the ruling.

Core Idea
Partial loss of bequest funds for freeing a slave: Abu Hanifa voids, Sahibayn execute like Hajj, differing on the nature of the act.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. A testator bequeaths that a slave be purchased and freed with 100 dirhams. After death, some dirhams are lost. Abu Hanifa rules that the bequest does not take effect with the remaining money, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, rule that it does, analogizing to a bequest for Hajj. The difference hinges on whether freeing a slave is a pure act of worship (right of Allah) or one that varies with the slave's value (right of the slave). We will analyse the reasoning and the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
State the case
Identify the bequest scenario: a testator bequeaths that a slave be purchased and freed with 100 dirhams. After his death, one dirham is lost.
2
State the two positions
Abu Hanifa: the bequest does not take effect with the remaining dirhams. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: it does take effect with the remainder, as in Hajj.
This is the crux of the dispute.
3
Examine the qiyas of the Sahibayn
The Sahibayn analogize to a bequest for Hajj with a specified sum: if some is lost, the remainder is used because Hajj is a pure worship owed to Allah. They argue freeing a slave is also a type of worship (qurbah), so it should be implemented as far as possible.
The basis of the analogy is that both are acts of worship.
4
Examine Abu Hanifa's distinction
Abu Hanifa rejects the analogy because the reward of freeing a slave varies with the slave's value. Using less money would free a cheaper slave, changing the intended act. Hajj, in contrast, is a pure act whose obligation does not change with the amount spent.
Key distinction: qurbah mutafawwitah (varying worship) vs qurbah mahdah (pure worship).
5
Trace the underlying principle
The Hidayah reveals a deeper basis: according to Abu Hanifa, freeing a slave is a right of the slave (the slave is the beneficiary), so changing the sum changes the beneficiary. According to the Sahibayn, it is a right of Allah, so the beneficiary (Allah) does not change, similar to Hajj.
This principle affects other areas, e.g., testimony without claim.

This is according to Abu Hanifa, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) the freeing takes effect with what remains as in Hajj. Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute about a specific bequest: a person bequeaths that a slave be purchased and freed with 100 dirhams. After his death, some dirhams are lost. Abu Hanifa rules that the bequest does not execute with the remaining money because the testator specified a hundred, and freeing a slave is an act of worship whose reward varies with the slave's value—freeing a cheaper slave would be a different act. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) analogize it to a bequest for Hajj: if some funds are lost, the remaining money is used because the act of worship is pure and the entitlement does not change. Abu Hanifa distinguishes: Hajj is a pure act of worship owed to Allah, so the obligation remains the same; freeing a slave is a right of the slave (according to him), so changing the beneficiary is not allowed. The Hidayah notes a deeper underlying principle: whether freeing is a right of Allah or of the slave. According to the companions, it is Allah's right, so the beneficiary doesn't change; according to Abu Hanifa, it is the slave's right, so the beneficiary does change. The text concludes that the description of the bequest (a slave worth a hundred) makes this interpretation more appropriate. Thus, the difference hinges on the nature of the act and the specificity of the bequest. This passage serves as an exercise in parsing the layers of legal reasoning and the subtle distinctions between different types of worship.

Key Terms
عتق ('itq): Freeing a slave; an act of worship whose reward varies with the value of the slave.
حج (hajj): Pilgrimage to Mecca; a pure act of worship owed to Allah, whose obligation does not change with the amount spent.
قربة (qurbah): An act of worship that brings one closer to Allah. In this context, the differentiation between acts that are pure (like hajj) and those that vary (like freeing a slave).
وصية (wasiyyah): A bequest made by a testator, valid from one-third of the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because freeing a slave is not a valid act of worship.
B) Because the reward of freeing a slave varies with the slave's value, so a cheaper slave changes the intended act.
C) Because the bequest for freeing a slave is always invalid if any money is lost.
D) Because the Sahibayn agree with Abu Hanifa on this issue.

Key Takeaway
The execution of a bequest for freeing a slave upon partial loss of funds depends on whether the act is considered a right of Allah (Sahibayn) or a right of the slave (Abu Hanifa).

Bequest to Free a Slave: Effect of Post-Death Crime (Daf' vs Fida')
Ruling on bequest to free a slave: effect of post-death crime.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the effects of paying blood money (daf') and ransoming (fida') on a bequest to free a slave, and state the underlying reasoning based on ownership transfer.

Core Idea
Payment transfers ownership and voids the bequest; ransom preserves it because the slave is purified.

This lesson examines a nuanced scenario in Hanafi inheritance law: a testator bequeaths that his slave be freed after his death, but before the slave is freed, he commits a crime requiring compensation. The heirs have two options, and their choice determines whether the bequest survives. We will analyze the ruling and its legal reasoning, focusing on the hierarchy of rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Understand the initial situation: a testator makes a bequest to free his slave after his death. Before the slave is freed, he commits a crime (jinayah) that imposes a financial liability (blood money). The heirs must address this liability.
The crime occurs after death but before the bequest is executed.
2
Understand the two options
The heirs have two choices: daf' (دفع) – pay the blood money to the victim's guardian, which transfers the slave as compensation; or fida' (فدى) – ransom the slave by paying the blood money from their own wealth, keeping the slave in the estate.
Daf' is a payment that transfers ownership; fida' is a voluntary ransom that purifies the slave.
3
Trace the legal effect
If daf' is chosen, the slave leaves the testator's ownership because the payment is valid and the victim's right takes precedence. Consequently, the bequest becomes void, analogous to a sale of the slave after death. If fida' is chosen, the slave is purified from the crime (as if he never committed it), so the bequest remains valid and is executed.
The key principle: the right of the victim (wali al-jinayah) is superior to the right of the testator and legatee.
4
Apply the reasoning
The reasoning (talil) is that daf' causes the slave to exit the testator's ownership, thereby voiding the bequest just as a sale would. Fida' does not transfer ownership; it only clears the liability, so the slave remains in the estate and the bequest proceeds.
The analogy to sale is explicit in the commentary.
5
Formulate the rule
The rule: A bequest to free a slave is void if the heirs discharge the slave's crime by paying blood money (daf') because the slave leaves the testator's ownership. It remains valid if the heirs ransom the slave (fida') from their own wealth.
This rule illustrates the hierarchy of claims: victim's compensation > testator's bequest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتبطل الوصية بعتق عبده إن جَنى بعد موته ، فدفع، وإن فدى لا) ، أوصى بأن يحتق الورثة عبده بعد موته ، فجنى العبد فدفع، بطلت الوصية ؛ لأنَّ الدفع قد صح ، فخرج عن ملكه، فبطلت الوصية، أما إن فدى الورثة كان الفداء في مالهم ؛ لأنهم التزموه ، فجازت الوصية ؛ لأنه طَهُرَ عن الجناية. [1] قوله : وتبطل الوصية ... الخ؛ لأنّ الدفع قد صح لما أن حق ولي الجناية مقدم على حق الموصي ، فكذلك على حق الموصى له ؛ لأنه يتلقى الملك من جهته إلا أن ملكه باق، وإنما يزولُ بالدفع، فإذا خرج به عن ملكه بطلت الوصية، كما إذا باعه الموصي أو الورثة بعد موته. كذا في «الهداية»). [2] قوله : وإن فدى ؛ أي لا تبطل الوصيّة ؛ لأنّ العبد طهر عن الجناية بالفداء، كأنه لم يجن فتنفذ الوصية، وكان الفداء في مالهم كما صرح به الشارح.
And the bequest to free his slave becomes void if the slave commits a crime after his death, and they pay (the blood money), and if they ransom (it does not become void). He bequeathed that the heirs free his slave after his death, then the slave committed a crime, and they paid: the bequest becomes void because the payment is valid, so the slave leaves his ownership, so the bequest becomes void. As for if the heirs ransomed, the ransom is from their wealth because they undertook it, so the bequest is valid because the slave becomes free of the crime. [1] His statement: 'And the bequest becomes void...' because the payment is valid because the right of the guardian of the crime takes precedence over the right of the testator, and likewise over the right of the legatee; because he receives ownership from him, except that his ownership remains, and it only ceases by payment. So when the slave leaves his ownership, the bequest becomes void, as if the testator or the heirs sold him after his death. As in al-Hidayah. [2] His statement: 'and if they ransom' i.e., the bequest does not become void because the slave is purified from the crime by the ransom, as if he had not committed a crime, so the bequest is executed, and the ransom is from their wealth as the commentator stated.
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with its underlying reasoning. It addresses a situation where a testator bequeaths that his slave be freed after his death, but before the slave is freed, the slave commits a crime (like murder, requiring payment of blood money). The heirs have two options: either pay the blood money to the victim's guardian (دفع), or ransom the slave (فدى). If the heirs pay the blood money, the slave is transferred to the victim's guardian as compensation, thus leaving the testator's ownership, and the bequest becomes void. If the heirs instead ransom the slave by paying the blood money from their own pockets, the slave remains in the estate and is purified from the crime, so the bequest is valid and the slave is freed. The commentary clarifies that the payment extinguishes the testator's ownership because the right of the victim's guardian is superior to the right of the testator (and the legatee). Thus, the bequest is analogous to a situation where the slave is sold by the testator or heirs after death. The ransom is a voluntary act by the heirs, so the slave remains in the estate and the bequest proceeds. This lesson illustrates the hierarchy of rights: the victim's right to compensation takes precedence over the testator's bequest.

Key Terms
دفع (daf'): Payment of blood money by the heirs to the victim's guardian, transferring the slave as compensation.
فدى (fida'): Ransom paid by the heirs from their own wealth to free the slave from liability, keeping him in the estate.
جناية (jinayah): A crime, especially one involving bodily harm or murder, requiring compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The bequest is still valid, but the slave is not freed.
B) The bequest becomes void because the slave leaves the testator's ownership.
C) The bequest is modified to free the slave with a different form.
D) The heirs must sell the slave to pay the blood money.

Key Takeaway
The right of the crime victim takes precedence over the bequest; only ransoming from the heirs' own wealth preserves the bequest.

Dispute Over Time of Manumission: Heir vs Legatee
Ruling on conflicting claims about timing of manumission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of attributing events to the nearest time to resolve disputes over whether a manumission occurred in health or death illness.

Core Idea
Events are attributed to the nearest time – the heir's claim prevails.

When a testator bequeaths one-third to a legatee (Zaid) and frees a slave, a dispute may arise: Zaid claims the slave was freed in health (so the freedom is unrestricted), while the heir claims it was freed during the death illness (so it is a bequest from one-third). The Hanafi school resolves this using a key evidentiary principle: events are attributed to the nearest possible time. Since the testator was in illness before death, the freeing is presumed to have occurred in that illness, giving the heir's claim priority. This lesson explores the ruling, its proof, and the exception when Zaid can still benefit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actors and their claims
Recognize the legatee (Zaid) and the heir, and what each claims about the timing of the slave's manumission.
Zaid claims 'sihhah' (health), heir claims 'marad' (death illness).
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The heir is believed (yusaddaq) and Zaid is deprived (yuhram) unless there is surplus from the one-third or Zaid provides proof.
3
Understand the proof: the attribution principle
The ruling is based on the principle 'al-hawadith tudafu ila aqrab al-awqat' (events are attributed to the nearest times). Since the testator was in illness immediately before death, the freeing is attributed to that illness.
This principle ensures certainty and is a Hanafi evidentiary tool.
4
Learn the exception
Zaid is not deprived if the one-third of the estate exceeds the value of the slave, or if he brings proof (bayyinah) that the freeing occurred in health.
The exception protects the legatee's right if the one-third is enough.
5
Apply to a concrete scenario
Given an estate value and slave value, determine whether Zaid receives anything and why.
Practice with numbers to solidify understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أوصى لزيد بثُلث ماله وتَرَكَ عبدا فادعى زيد عتقهُ في صحتِه ، والوارث في مرضه، صُدِّقَ الوارث ، وحُرمَ زيد إلا أن يفضل عن ثلثه شيء، أو يُبرهن على دعواه. [3] قوله : فيحرم زيد.... الخ ; ولأن العتق حادث، والحوادث تضاف إلى أقرب الأوقات للتيقن بها، فكان الظاهر شاهداً للوارث، فيكون القول قوله مع اليمين إلا أن يكون ثلث المال زائداً على قيمة العبد... الخ.
If he bequeaths to Zaid a third of his property and leaves a slave, and Zaid claims that he (the testator) freed the slave in health, and the heir claims (he freed him) in his illness, the heir is believed, and Zaid is deprived unless there is surplus from the one-third, or he provides proof. [3] His statement: 'Zaid is deprived...' because freeing is an event, and events are attributed to the nearest times for certainty, so the apparent circumstance testifies for the heir, so his statement is accepted with an oath except if the one-third of the property exceeds the value of the slave... etc.
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on a dispute between a legatee (Zaid) and an heir. The testator bequeathed one-third of his property to Zaid and also freed a slave. Zaid claims the slave was freed in health (so the freedom is effective from the full estate, not restricted to one-third), while the heir claims it was freed in the death illness (so it is treated as a bequest from the one-third). The heir is believed because events are attributed to the nearest possible time: since the testator was in illness before death, the freeing is presumed to have occurred in that illness. Zaid is thus deprived of a share in the slave's value unless the one-third of the estate exceeds the slave's value, in which case the surplus goes to Zaid, or if Zaid can prove his claim. The commentary adds that the heir's statement is accepted with an oath. This ruling balances the principle of attributing events to the nearest time (a rule of evidence) with the rights of the legatee. It also illustrates the Hanafi rule that a bequest (including a manumission in death illness) is limited to one-third of the estate.

Key Terms
صحة (sihhah): State of health; actions in health take full effect from the entire estate.
مرض (marad): Death illness; actions during death illness are treated as bequests and limited to one-third.
ظاهر (zahir): Apparent circumstance; the presumption that events occur at the nearest time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When Zaid provides proof that the freeing was in health.
B) When the slave is very valuable.
C) When the heir admits Zaid is right.
D) When the testator had no other debts.

Key Takeaway
In a dispute over whether a manumission occurred in health or death illness, the Hanafi school believes the heir because events are attributed to the nearest time, unless the legatee proves otherwise or the one-third has surplus.

Annulment of Bequest When Slave Is Delivered for Homicide
Ruling on annulment of bequest when slave is delivered after homicide

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a testamentary emancipation when the slave commits homicide after the testator's death, and explain the conditions that annul or preserve the bequest.

Core Idea
Delivery annuls bequest; ransom preserves it.

When a testator bequeaths emancipation of a slave, and that slave commits homicide after the testator's death, the legal outcome depends on the heirs' response. This lesson dissects the ruling from al-Hidayah, clarifying when the bequest stands or falls. We will trace the reasoning behind the annulment and the conditions that keep the bequest valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and two scenarios
The act is a testamentary emancipation (wasiyyah to free a slave). The passage presents two scenarios: the slave is delivered to the victim's guardian (daf'), or the heirs ransom the slave (fida').
2
Analyse the first scenario: delivery
If the slave is delivered, the bequest is annulled because the slave leaves the testator's ownership, just as if sold. The victim's right takes precedence over the testator's right.
Annulment is due to complete transfer of ownership.
3
Analyse the second scenario: ransom
If the heirs pay ransom from their own wealth, the bequest remains valid because the slave is purified from the crime, as if it never happened. The ransom is not from the estate but from the heirs.
Validity requires ransom to be paid by heirs, not from the slave or estate.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The right of the victim's guardian (wali al-jinaya) is prioritized over the testator's right. Delivery removes the slave from ownership, annulling the bequest. Ransom preserves the slave's purity and thus the bequest.
Priority of rights is a key Hanafi principle in this chapter.
5
Note the analogy with sale
The commentary analogizes delivery to a sale of the slave by the testator or heirs after death, which would also annul the bequest. This shows that any transfer of ownership after death annuls a testamentary disposition of that property.
The illa is removal from ownership after death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتبطل الوصية بعتق عبده إن جَنّى بعد موته ، فدفع، وإن فدى لا. وتبطل الوصية بعتق عبده إن جَنَى بعد موته ، فدفع، وإن فدى لا)، أوصى بأن يحتق الورثة عبده بعد موته ، فجنَى العبد فدفع، بطلت الوصية ؛ لأنَّ الدفع قد صح ، فخرج عن ملكه، فبطلت الوصية، أما إن فَدَى الورثة كان الفداء في مالهم ؛ لأنهم التزموه ، فجازت الوصية ؛ لأنه طَهُرَ عن الجناية. قوله : وتبطل الوصية ... الخ ؛ لأنّ الدفع قد صح لما أن حق ولي الجناية مقدم على حق الموصي ، فكذلك على حق الموصى له ؛ لأنه يتلقى الملك من جهته إلا أن ملكه باق، وإنما يزولُ بالدفع، فإذا خرج به عن ملكه بطلت الوصية، كما إذا باعه الموصي أو الورثة بعد موته. كذا في «الهداية». قوله : وإن فدى ؛ أي لا تبطل الوصيّة ؛ لأنّ العبد طهر عن الجناية بالفداء، كأنه لم يجن فتنفذ الوصية، وكان الفداء في مالهم كما صرح به الشارح إلا أن.
And the bequest of emancipating his slave is annulled if the slave commits homicide after his death, so he is delivered, and if ransomed, no. And the bequest of emancipating his slave is annulled if the slave commits homicide after his death, so he is delivered, and if ransomed, no), he instructed that the heirs retain his slave after his death, so the slave committed homicide and they delivered him, the bequest was annulled because the delivery was effective, so he left his ownership, thus the bequest was annulled. However, if the heirs ransomed him, the ransom is from their wealth because they undertook it, so the bequest is valid because he became purified from the crime. He said: And the bequest is annulled... etc.; because the delivery was effective since the right of the guardian of the crime takes precedence over the right of the testator, and likewise over the right of the legatee because he receives ownership from him, except that his ownership remains, and it is removed only by delivery, so when it leaves his ownership, the bequest is annulled, just as if the testator or heirs sold him after his death. Thus in al-Hidayah. He said: And if ransomed; meaning the bequest is not annulled because the slave becomes purified from the crime by the ransom, as if he did not commit the crime, so the bequest is executed, and the ransom is from their wealth as the commentator explicitly stated, except that.
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn with its commentary. The ruling concerns testamentary emancipation (a bequest to free a slave). If the slave commits homicide after the testator's death, the bequest is annulled if the slave is delivered (daf') to the victim's guardian, because the victim's right takes precedence and the slave leaves the testator's ownership. However, if the heirs ransom (fida') the slave from their own wealth, the bequest remains valid because the slave is purified from the crime. The commentator explains the reasoning: the right of the victim's guardian is given priority over the testator's right, and by extension over the legatee since the legatee acquires ownership from the testator. The delivery removes the slave from ownership, annulling the bequest analogously to a sale. Ransoming preserves the bequest because the slave becomes free of liability.

Key Terms
وصية (wasiyyah): Testamentary disposition; a bequest to free a slave (عتق) after death.
دفع (daf'): Delivery of the slave to the victim's guardian as compensation for homicide.
فداء (fida'): Ransom paid by heirs to free the slave from criminal liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Credibility in Disputed Emancipation: Heir vs. Legatee
Ruling on credibility in disputed emancipation timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on who is believed when a legatee claims emancipation in health and the heir claims illness, and explain the underlying principle of attributing events to the nearest time.

Core Idea
Events are attributed to the nearest time; thus the heir's claim of illness-default is believed.

This lesson examines a specific conflict in bequests: when a testator both bequeaths one-third to a legatee and emancipates a slave. After death, the legatee claims the emancipation occurred in health (to protect his bequest) while the heir claims it occurred in the death-illness (making it a bequest from the one-third). The Hanafi school resolves this by a principle of attribution that gives the heir's claim precedence. We will work through the rule and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
The legatee (Zayd) claims the emancipation happened in health (sihha). The heir claims it happened in death-illness (marad al-mawt).
In health, emancipation is effective from the whole estate; in illness, it is limited to one-third.
2
Note the legal consequence of each claim
If the heir is correct, the emancipation is a bequest from one-third, which takes priority over any other bequest. If the legatee is correct, his bequest can be fulfilled from the full one-third without being reduced by the emancipation.
3
Apply the principle of attributing events to the nearest time
The commentator states that emancipation is an event, and events are attributed to the nearest possible time for certainty (a form of istishab). This makes the apparent evidence support the heir's claim.
Hanafi usul: al-asl baqa' ma kana 'ala ma kana – the presumption is continuity of the status quo.
4
Determine the outcome and exceptions
The heir's statement is accepted (with an oath), so Zayd is deprived unless either: (a) the one-third of the estate exceeds the slave's value, in which case Zayd gets the surplus, or (b) Zayd brings clear proof of his claim.
This protects the heir's right by default but allows exceptions when the facts justify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أوصى لزيد بثُلث ماله وتَرَكَ عبدا فادعى زيد عتقه في صحتِه ، والوارث في مرضه، صُدِّقَ الوارث ، وحُرِمَ زيد إلا أن يفضل عن ثلثه شيء، أو يُبرهن على دعواه. أوصى لزيد بثلث ماله وأعتق عبداً ، فادعى زيد ان الميت قد أعتق العبد في الصحة ؛ لئلا يكون وصيّة، فتنفذ وصيته من ثلث المال، وقال الوارث : أعتقه في مرضه، والعتق في المرض مقدم على الوصية بثلث المال ، فالقول للوارث ؛ لأنه يُنْكِرُ استحقاق زيدٍ فيحرم زيد ، إلا أن يكون ثلث المال زائداً على قيمة العبد، فتنفذ الوصية لزيدٍ فيما زادَ الثَّلث على القيمة ، أو يُبَرْهِنَ. قوله : فيحرم زيد.... الخ ؛ ولأن العتق حادث، والحوادث تضاف إلى أقرب الأوقات للتيقن بها، فكان الظاهر شاهداً للوارث، فيكون القول قوله مع اليمين إلا أن يكون ثلث المال زائداً على قيمة العبد... الخ.
If he bequeaths to Zayd one-third of his wealth and leaves a slave, and Zayd claims that he emancipated him during health, and the heir claims during illness, the heir is believed, and Zayd is deprived unless there is a surplus from the one-third, or he brings proof of his claim. He bequeathed to Zayd one-third of his wealth and emancipated a slave, so Zayd claimed that the deceased had emancipated the slave during health so that it would not be a bequest, so his bequest would be executed from one-third of the wealth, and the heir said: He emancipated him during his illness, and emancipation during illness takes precedence over the bequest of one-third, so the heir's statement is accepted because he denies Zayd's entitlement, so Zayd is deprived unless the one-third of the wealth exceeds the value of the slave, in which case the bequest is executed for Zayd in the excess over the value, or he brings proof. He said: so Zayd is deprived... etc.; and because emancipation is an event, and events are attributed to the nearest of times for certainty, so the apparent evidence supports the heir, so his statement is accepted with an oath unless the one-third exceeds the value of the slave... etc.
Source pages p. 615

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the matn and its commentary. The case: a testator bequeaths one-third of his estate to Zayd and also emancipates a slave. After death, Zayd claims the emancipation occurred during the testator's health (so it is not a bequest from one-third but a full emancipation from the estate, allowing the bequest to take effect free of the slave's value), while the heir claims it occurred during the death-illness (making it a bequest from one-third, which takes priority over Zayd's bequest). The Hanafi ruling: the heir's claim is believed because emancipation is an event, and legal events are attributed to the nearest possible time (a principle of istishab). Thus the emancipation is considered to have occurred in the illness, which takes precedence over the bequest of one-third to Zayd. Therefore Zayd is deprived unless the one-third of the estate exceeds the value of the slave, in which case he receives the surplus. If Zayd brings clear proof, his claim is accepted. The commentator adds that the heir's statement is accepted with an oath.

Key Terms
صحة (sihha): State of health before the death-illness; actions in health are not subject to the one-third limitation.
مرض (marad): Death-illness (marad al-mawt); a legal state where certain dispositions are considered bequests from one-third.
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave; in this context, a testamentary act that may be from health or illness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The slave, because he is directly affected.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of attributing events to the nearest time gives the heir's claim precedence over the legatee's in emancipation timing disputes, unless the legatee proves otherwise or the one-third exceeds the slave's value.

Debt vs. Emancipation: Conflict of Claims in Hanafi Law
Intra-Hanafi dispute on conflict between debt and emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding a slave's obligation to work when both debt and emancipation are acknowledged by the heir, and apply the ruling to a similar scenario.

Core Idea
When debt and emancipation conflict, the stronger claim (debt) overrides but cannot annul emancipation; the slave must work for his value.

In this lesson, we explore an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two illustrious students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). The scenario involves a deceased person's heir who acknowledges both a debt owed to a third party and the emancipation of the deceased's slave during health. At stake is whether the slave becomes immediately free or must work to pay his value. Understanding this dispute sharpens your skills in legal priority (ta'addul) and partial enforcement of conflicting claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the parties' claims
A person dies. A creditor claims a debt against the deceased. The deceased's slave claims that he was emancipated during health. The heir confirms both claims. Determine the legal analysis.
This is a classical illustration of conflicting rights in inheritance and emancipation.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof (dalil)
Abu Hanifa rules that the slave must work (sa'a) to pay his value. His reasoning: the acknowledgment of the debt is stronger because it comes from the entire estate, whereas emancipation during illness is limited to one-third. The stronger should prevail; since emancipation cannot be annulled after occurrence, its effect is reduced by requiring the slave to pay through work.
Note the principle: the stronger claim repels the weaker (al-aqwa yadfa' al-ad'af).
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the slave is immediately free without any obligation to work. Their reasoning: the debt and emancipation claims appear simultaneously through the heir's single statement of confirmation, so they are treated as occurring together. Emancipation during health never requires work (si'aya).
Their emphasis is on simultaneity: if both are acknowledged at once, neither has temporal priority.
4
Analyze the core legal difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, the slave is partially free and must work to pay his value; under the Sahibayn, he is fully free with no obligation. The practical consequence affects whether the creditor collects from the slave's work or from the estate only.
This difference arises from how each imam weighs the strength vs. simultaneity of the claims.
5
Determine the relied-upon position (mufta bihi)
Later Hanafi authorities tend to adopt Abu Hanifa's view, as reflected in many fatwa manuals. The text presents his reasoning in detail, indicating its weight.
The Zahir al-Riwayah contains both views, but tarjih favors the stronger proof of Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن ادعى رجل ديناً على ميت، وعبده إعتاقه في صحته، وصدقهما وارثه، سعى العبد في قيمته، هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وقالا : يعتق، ولا يسعى في شيء؛ لأنَّ الدِّين والعتق في الصحةِ ظهرا معاً بتصديق الوارث في كلام واحد، فصار كأنهما وقعا معا، والعتق في الصحة لا يوجب السعاية، له : أنّ الإقرار بالدين أقوى ؛ لأنه يعتبر من جميع المال، والإقرار بالعتق في المرض يعتبر من الثلث، والأقوى يدفعُ الأدنى، فيجب أن يبطل العتق أصلاً ؛ لكنه بعد وقوعه لا يحتمل البطلان فيدفعُ من حيث المعنى بإيجاب السعاية.
If a man claims a debt against the deceased, and his slave claims his emancipation during health, and the heir confirms both, the slave works for his value. This according to Abu Hanifa. And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He is free and does not work for anything, because the debt and the emancipation during health appeared together through the heir's confirmation in one statement, so it is as if they occurred together, and emancipation during health does not necessitate working. For him (Abu Hanifa): That acknowledgment of the debt is stronger because it is considered from the entire estate, while acknowledgment of emancipation during illness is considered from one-third, and the stronger repels the weaker, so the emancipation should be annulled entirely, but since after it occurs it cannot be annulled, it is repelled in meaning by imposing working.
Source pages p. 616

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani). The scenario: a deceased person has a slave, and two claims are made: (1) a man claims a debt against the deceased, (2) the slave claims he was emancipated during health. The heir confirms both claims. Abu Hanifa rules that the slave must work (sa'a) to pay his own value (i.e., he becomes partially free but must earn his freedom by working off his price). The sahibayn rule that the slave is fully free without any obligation to work (sa'aya). Their reasoning: both the debt and the emancipation claim are confirmed by the heir in one statement, so they are considered simultaneous, and emancipation during health does not require working for value. Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the acknowledgment of the debt is stronger because it is paid from the entire estate, whereas emancipation during illness is limited to one-third; the stronger should prevail. Ideally, the emancipation should be annulled, but since it has already occurred and cannot be annulled, it is effectively negated by imposing the requirement of paying the value through working.

Key Terms
سعاية (si'aya): Working to pay the value of the slave's freedom; a form of partial emancipation.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession by the heir regarding the claims.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens when a deceased's slave claims emancipation and a third party claims a debt, and the heir confirms both?
A) The slave is free with no obligation.
B) The slave must work to pay his value.
C) The debt is cancelled.
D) The emancipation is invalid.

Key Takeaway
When two claims of different strengths conflict, the stronger claim overrides but does not fully annul the weaker; instead, the weaker is enforced partially, as seen in the requirement for the slave to work for his value.

Who is a Neighbor? Defining Jar for Bequests
Definition and internal Hanafi dispute on the meaning of 'neighbor' for bequests, with arguments from qiyas and istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi definitions of 'jar' (neighbor) for bequests and apply them to determine who benefits from a bequest 'to my neighbors'.

Core Idea
For bequests, Abu Hanifa defines neighbor as immediate adjacent; Sahibayn include all in the mosque community.

When a testator makes a bequest 'to my neighbors,' who exactly qualifies? Is it only the person living next door, or does it include the whole community? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). This passage from the chapter on bequests clarifies the definitions and the reasoning behind each position: qiyas (literal adjacency) versus istihsan (customary inclusion). Understanding this ikhtilaf is essential for correctly executing a bequest to neighbors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
The passage defines 'jar' in the context of a bequest to neighbors. There are two positions: Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's position
According to Abu Hanifa, a neighbor is only the person whose property directly adjoins the testator's property (mulāṣiq). This is based on the literal meaning of 'jara' and qiyas (analogy) with the right of pre-emption (shuf'a), which also depends on adjacency.
He argues that since it is impossible to include all possible neighbors, the term is restricted to the most specific category.
3
Understand the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn hold that 'jar' includes all residents of the same locality who are united by the same mosque. This is istihsan (juristic preference) based on custom ('urf), supported by the hadith: 'No prayer for the neighbor of the mosque except in the mosque,' interpreted as all who hear the call.
They consider the testator's desirability to include those with whom he intermingles daily.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, only immediate neighbors share the bequest equally, resulting in fewer beneficiaries and larger shares. Under the Sahibayn, the bequest is divided among all locality residents sharing the mosque, increasing the number of beneficiaries and reducing each share.
5
Apply to a scenario
Given a testator who lives in a house with four adjacent neighbours, and a mosque shared by the entire block of 20 houses, determine who inherits under each definition. Also note that the Sahibayn's inclusive category includes owners and residents, male/female, Muslim/dhimmi, but there is a dispute about a resident slave.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الوصية للأقارب وغيرهم
جاره : من لصق به، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ، وعندهما: الملاصق وغيره سواء.
[1]قوله : باب الوصية للأقارب.... الخ ؛ لَمّا كان هذا الباب مشتملاً على الوصايا بالقوم المخصوصين أخَّر ذكره عن الأبواب المتقدّمة ؛ لقلة فائدته ؛ لأنّ ما تقدم لا يختص بقوم دون قوم، فكان لعموم فائدته تقديمه ذكراً أولى. كذا في «غاية البيان».
[2]قوله : جاره ... الخ ؛ أي من أوصى لجيرانه فهم الملاصقون به ؛ لأنّ جاره من لصق به ؛ أي اتصل داره بدار الموصي ، قربت الأبواب أو بعدت، رجالاً كانوا أو نساء، ذميين كانوا أو مسلمين، فالوصية بينهم على السوية.
[3] قوله : هذا عند أبي حنيفة الله ؛ وهو القياس ؛ لأنّ الجار من المجاورة، وهي الملاصقة حقيقة ؛ ولهذا يستحق الشفعة بهذا الجوار ؛ ولأنه لَمَّا تعدَّرَ صرفُهُ إلى الجميع يصرف إلى أخص الخصوص وهو الملاصق.
[4] قوله : وغيره ؛ ممن يسكن في محلّة الموصي، ويجمعهم مسجد المحلة، وهذا استحسان ؛ لأنّ هؤلاء كلهم يُسمون جيراناً عرفاً، وقد يؤيد هذا بقول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : لا صلاة لجار المسجد إلا في المسجد وفسره عليه بكلِّ مَن سمع النداء ؛ ولأنَّ مقصد الموصي من الوصية هـو الجيران، واستحبابه ينتظم الملاصق وغيره، إلا أنه لا بدّ من الاختلاط، وذلك عند اتحاد المسجد ، قال في «الهداية» : قالوا : ويستوي فيه الساكن والمالك، والذكر والأنثى، والمسلم والذمي ؛ لأنَّ اسم الجار يتناولهم ، وفي العبد الساكن اختلاف.
Chapter on bequest to relatives and others.
His neighbor: one who adheres to him. This is according to Abu Hanifa. And according to the two (Sahibayn): the adjacent and others are equal.
[1] The remark: 'Chapter on bequest to relatives, etc.' Since this chapter contains bequests to specific groups, it is placed after the preceding chapters due to its lesser benefit; because what precedes is not specific to one group, so for generality of benefit, it is given precedence in mention. Likewise in Ghayat al-Bayan.
[2] The remark: 'His neighbor, etc.' That is, whoever makes a bequest to his neighbors, they are those adjacent to him, because his neighbor is one who adheres to him, i.e., whose house adjoins the testator's house, whether doors are close or far, whether men or women, dhimmis or Muslims. So the bequest is divided equally among them.
[3] The remark: 'This is according to Abu Hanifa' — and it is qiyas, because 'neighbor' derives from adjacence (mujawara), which is literal adjacency. For this reason, the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) is established by this adjacency. And since it is impossible to include everyone, it is restricted to the most specific, which is the adjacent.
[4] The remark: 'and others' — meaning those who live in the testator's locality and are united by the mosque of the locality. This is istihsan, because all such people are called neighbors customarily ('urfan). This is supported by the saying of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him): 'There is no prayer for the neighbor of the mosque except in the mosque,' interpreted as everyone who hears the call. And because the testator's intention in the bequest is the neighbors, and his desirability includes the adjacent and others. However, there must be intermingling, which occurs by unity of mosque. He said in al-Hidayah: 'They said: The resident and the owner, male and female, Muslim and dhimmi are equal in this, because the name 'neighbor' includes them all. Regarding a resident slave, there is disagreement.'
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on bequests (wasaya) to relatives and others, focusing specifically on the definition of 'neighbor' (jar) for the purpose of a bequest 'to my neighbors.' The text first states the two positions: According to Abu Hanifa, 'neighbor' means only those whose house adjoins the testator's house (immediate adjacency). According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the adjacent and others (e.g., those in the same locality sharing a mosque) are all included equally. The commentary then expands on each point. The first note explains why this chapter is placed after earlier chapters: it deals with specific groups, while previous chapters dealt with general rulings, so the latter are given precedence due to generality of benefit. The second note defines 'jar' as one who adheres (al-ṣaqa = attaches) by physical contiguity of houses, regardless of distance of doors, gender, or religion. The bequest is divided equally among such immediate neighbors. The third note explains Abu Hanifa's view as qiyas (analogy): the literal meaning of 'jar' is adjacency, and the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) also depends on adjacency. Since the testator's bequest cannot be practically extended to all possible neighbors (too many), it is restricted to the most particular category, which is the immediately adjacent. The fourth note presents the Sahibayn's view as istihsan (juristic preference): custom ('urf) calls all residents of the same mosque community 'neighbors.' This is supported by the hadith 'No prayer for the neighbor of the mosque except in the mosque,' which is interpreted to include all who hear the call (adhan). The testator's likely intention is to benefit all neighbors with whom he intermingles daily, not just those attached to his wall. The commentary notes that in al-Hidayah, it is stated that all resident owners, males and females, Muslims and dhimmis are equal in this status because the name 'jar' includes them, but there is disagreement regarding a resident slave (whether he counts as a neighbor).

Key Terms
جوار (jiwār): Neighborhood or adjacence; in this context, the basis for determining who is a neighbor.
ملاصق (mulāṣiq): The immediately adjacent property owner; the one whose house touches the testator's house.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that departs from strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more equitable or customary solution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the Sahibayn's definition of 'jar' in bequests?
A) Strict qiyas from the law of pre-emption
B) Istihsan based on custom and a hadith
C) Ijma of the companions
D) Literal meaning of the word 'jara'

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of 'neighbor' for bequests depends on the chosen method: qiyas (immediate adjacency) or istihsan (locality and mosque community), with no single mufta bihi explicitly stated.

Definition of Neighbor in a Will
Defines 'neighbor' for wills; presents Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's definitions of 'neighbor' in a will, and state the relied-upon position with its basis.

Core Idea
In a will for neighbors, Abu Hanifa restricts to adjoining properties; the Sahibayn include all in the locality — the relied-upon view follows custom.

When someone makes a bequest 'to my neighbors', who exactly is included? This seemingly simple question sparked an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples. The answer reveals how the school balances literal analogy (qiyas) against customary usage ('urf) via istihsan. Let's examine the reasoning behind each position and see which one ultimately prevailed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Issue
The issue is the definition of 'jar' (neighbor) in the context of a will. The literal meaning is 'adjoining', but custom may extend it. Determine what scope the testator likely intended.
The passage focuses on fiqh interpretation of a single word.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position
Abu Hanifa holds that 'neighbor' means only those whose property directly adjoins the testator's (mulāṣiq). This is based on qiyas from the literal meaning and the rule of preemption (shuf'a).
Qiyas here is a strict linguistic analogy.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that 'neighbor' includes all residents of the testator's locality who share a common mosque. This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) with customary usage and a supporting hadith.
Istihsan overrides qiyas when custom dictates a broader meaning.
4
Examine the Proofs
Abu Hanifa's proof: qiyas based on adjacency, and the impossibility of applying to all. Sahibayn's proof: 'urf (custom), the hadith about the mosque neighbor, and the testator's likely intention to include all who mix socially.
The hadith is used to show that 'neighbor' can extend beyond mere adjacency.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, only adjoining property owners share the bequest; under the Sahibayn, all residents of the locality (who share the mosque) share equally.
The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's inclusive position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جاره : من لصق به . هذا عند أبي حنيفة ، وعندهما: الملاصق وغيره سواء.
قوله : جاره ... الخ ؛ أي من أوصى لجيرانه فهم الملاصقون به ؛ لأنّ جاره من لصق به ؛ أي اتصل داره بدار الموصي ، قربت الأبواب أو بعدت، رجالاً كانوا أو نساء، ذميين كانوا أو مسلمين، فالوصية بينهم على السوية.
قوله : هذا عند أبي حنيفة الله ؛ وهو القياس ؛ لأنّ الجار من المجاورة، وهي الملاصقة حقيقة ؛ ولهذا يستحق الشفعة بهذا الجوار ؛ ولأنه لَمَّا تعدَّرَ صرفُهُ إلى الجميع يصرف إلى أخص الخصوص وهو الملاصق.
قوله : وغيره ؛ ممن يسكن في محلّة الموصي، ويجمعهم مسجد المحلة، وهذا استحسان ؛ لأنّ هؤلاء كلهم يُسمون جيراناً عرفاً، وقد يؤيد هذا بقول رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : لا صلاة لجار المسجد إلا في المسجد وفسره عليه بكلِّ مَن سمع النداء ؛ ولأنَّ مقصد الموصي من الوصية هـو الجيران، واستحبابه ينتظم الملاصق وغيره، إلا أنه لا بدّ من الاختلاط، وذلك عند اتحاد المسجد ، قال في «الهداية» : قالوا : ويستوي فيه الساكن والمالك، والذكر والأنثى، والمسلم والذمي ؛ لأنَّ اسم الجار يتناولهم ، وفي العبد الساكن اختلاف.
His neighbor: whoever adjoins him. This is according to Abu Hanifa, and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): the adjoining and others are equal. He said: 'His neighbor...' i.e., whoever makes a will for his neighbors, they are those who adjoin him; because his neighbor is whoever adjoins him, i.e., his house connects to the house of the testator, whether the doors are near or far, whether they are men or women, dhimmis or Muslims, so the bequest is divided equally among them. He said: 'This is according to Abu Hanifa' — and this is analogy (qiyas); because neighbor (jar) is from adjacency (mujawara), and it is literal adjacency; and for this reason preemption (shuf'a) is established by this adjacency; and because when it is impossible to apply it to all, it is applied to the most specific, which is the adjoining. He said: 'And other' — i.e., whoever lives in the testator's locality and they share a mosque of the locality. This is istihsan (juristic preference); because all of these are called neighbors customarily ('urfan), and this is supported by the saying of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him): 'No prayer for the neighbor of the mosque except in the mosque' and he explained it as everyone who hears the call; and because the testator's intention in the bequest is the neighbors, and his recommendation includes both the adjoining and others, except that there must be mixing (ikhtilat), and that is when the mosque is shared. He said in al-Hidayah: They said: In this, the resident and the owner, the male and the female, the Muslim and the dhimmi are equal; because the name 'neighbor' applies to them all. Regarding the residing slave, there is a difference of opinion.
Source pages p. 618

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the definition of 'neighbor' (jar) in the context of a will. The base matn states that a neighbor is the one who adjoins (mulasiq) the testator's property, which is the position of Abu Hanifa. The two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, hold that both the adjoining and non-adjoining residents of the same locality are included. The commentary explains the reasoning: Abu Hanifa relies on qiyas (analogy) based on the literal meaning of 'jar' (from mujawara, adjacency) and the rule of preemption (shuf'a) which also requires adjacency. The Sahibayn prefer istihsan (juristic preference) based on customary usage ('urf), supported by the hadith about the mosque neighbor and the testator's likely intention to include all those who mix in the mosque. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the Sahibayn's inclusive view, as it accords with custom and the hadith. The passage also notes equality among residents regardless of gender, religion, or ownership, except for a slave who resides there, about which there is disagreement.

Key Terms
جار (jār): The person or persons named as beneficiaries in a will for 'neighbors'.
ملاصق (mulāṣiq): The adjoining property owner whose house touches the testator's.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in favor of custom and broader benefit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Istihsan based on customary usage and a hadith.
C) Ijma of the companions.
D) Strict adherence to lexical definitions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines 'neighbor' in a will according to custom ('urf), not literal adjacency, through the principle of istihsan.
