Bequest to Orphans, Blind, etc.: Enumerability and Distribution
Ruling and definitions for bequests to specific needy groups

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on bequests to countable vs uncountable groups of orphans, blind, etc., and apply the definitions of yatim and armal to determine distribution.

Core Idea
If the group can be counted, all members share; if not, only the poor receive—charity governs when exact ownership is impossible.

A bequest to a specific category of people, such as orphans or widows of a certain lineage, raises the question of how to distribute. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on whether the group can be precisely counted. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the definitions of key terms, and the scholarly differences within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories and the basic ruling
Locate the categories mentioned: orphans of his sons, blind, crippled, widows. The general rule: if they are countable (yuhsawn), all members, rich and poor, male and female, share. If not countable, only the poor receive.
The distinction hinges on the possibility of ownership (tamlik) vs charity (qurbah).
2
Understand the condition of enumerability (ahsaw)
The text discusses what it means for a group to be 'countable'. Abu Yusuf says they can be counted without a written record; Muhammad says if over 100 they are not countable; the relied view delegates to the judge's discretion.
This intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf affects practical distribution.
3
Define 'yatim' (orphan)
The commentary defines yatim as a minor whose father has died, citing the hadith 'There is no orphanhood after puberty.' This excludes adults from the bequest if the wording is strictly 'orphans'.
The proof is from Sunan Abi Dawud.
4
Define 'armal' (widow/poor)
Armal is a poor person without a spouse. The relied view (Muhammad) specifies a woman who had a husband and then lost him. Others include any woman without a husband regardless of prior marriage.
The definition affects whether wealthy widows are included.
5
Synthesize the distribution rule
Combine the definitions and enumerability: if the group is countable, all individuals specified by the testator's words share equally. If not countable, only the poor among them receive because the intent is charity (sadd al-hajah).
The testator's words and context matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي أيتام بنيه، وعميانهم ، وزمناهم، وأراملهم، دخل فقيرهم وغنيهم، وذَكَرُهم وإناتهم إن احصوا، وإلا فللفقراء منهم . [2] قوله : وفي أيتام بنيه؛ أي لو أوصى لأيتام بني فلان أو لعميانهم أو لزمناهم أو لأراملهم إن كانوا قوما يحصون دخل في الوصية فقيرهم وغنّيهم، ذكورهم وإناثهم؛ لأنه أمكن تحقيق التمليك في حقهم، والوصية تمليك وإلا أي وإن كانوا لا يحصلون، فالوصية في الفقراء منهم؛ لأنَّ المقصود من الوصيّة القربة، وهي في سدّ الحاجة، وردّ الجوعة، وهذه الأسامي تشعر بتحقيق الحاجة، فجاز حمله على الفقراء (۳). اعلم أن الأيتام جمع يتيم : وهو اسم لصغير مات أبوه؛ لقول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: «لا يتم بعد الحلم)، رواه أبو داود في سننه»؛ ولأنَّ اليتيم عبارة عن الانفراد : يقال : درة تيمية؛ أي لا نظير لها منفردة، فمن انفرد عمن يربيه في حال حاجته إلى التربية كان يتيماً، وبعد البلوغ قد استغنى عمن يربيه لقدرته على القيام بمصالح نفسه فلا يسمى يتيماً. والأرامل جمع أرمل، في «التهذيب»: يقال للفقير الذي لا يقدر على شيء من رجل أو امرأة أرمل، ولا يقال : التي لا زوج لها وهي موسرة أرملة. وقال الشعبي : الأنوثة ليست بشرط، بل يدخل فيه الذكر والأنثى إلا أنّ الصحيح ما فسره محمد الله أن الأرملة : هي المرأة البالغة التي كان لها زوج فارقها أو مات عنها، دخل بها أو لم يدخل، وقوله حجة في اللغة. وقال بعضهم: الأرملة التي لا زوج لها ولا يشترط أنه كان لها زوج. كذا في «الكفاية»(۲). فإن كانوا قوماً يحصون دَخَلَ الفقيرُ والغني ، فإنه يكون تمليكاً لهم، وإن كانوا قوماً لا يحصون، لا يكون تمليكا لهم، بل يراد به القربة، وهي في دفع الحاجة، فيصرفُ إلى الفقراء منهم : أي فقراء أيتام بني زيد، أو فقراء عميانهم ، وكذا في الباقي [١] قوله : قوما يحصون؛ تفسيره عند أبي يوسف له أن يحصون بغير كتاب وحساب، وقال محمد : إذا كانوا أكثر من مئة فهم لا يحصون، وقال بعضهم : مفوض إلى رأي القاضي، وعليه الفتوى ، وإليه يشير ما قاله محمد ه. كذا في «الكفاية» ناقلا عن «فتاوی قاضي خان».
And in orphans of his sons, and their blind, and their crippled, and their widows: enter their poor and their rich, and their males and females if they are counted, otherwise for the poor among them. [2] His statement: 'And in orphans of his sons' meaning if one makes a bequest to orphans of the sons of so-and-so, or to their blind, or to their crippled, or to their widows, if they are a people who can be counted, then the bequest includes their poor and their rich, their males and their females; because it is possible to achieve ownership for them, and the bequest is ownership. Otherwise, meaning if they cannot be counted, then the bequest is for the poor among them; because the purpose of the bequest is closeness (to God), and it lies in filling need and repelling hunger, and these names indicate the fulfillment of need, so it is permissible to restrict it to the poor. (3) Know that 'aytam' is the plural of 'yatim', which is a name for a minor whose father died, due to the Prophet's saying: 'There is no orphanhood after puberty,' reported by Abu Dawud in his Sunan; and because 'yatim' is an expression for being alone: it is said 'durrah yatimah' meaning a unique pearl with no peer, so whoever is alone from one who raises him in a time of need for upbringing is an orphan, and after puberty he becomes independent of a caretaker due to his ability to manage his own affairs, so he is not called an orphan. 'Aramil' is plural of 'armal'. In al-Tahdhib: 'armal' is said for a poor person, male or female, who cannot afford anything; it is not said for a wealthy woman without a husband. Al-Sha'bi said: femininity is not a condition; rather it includes male and female, but the correct interpretation by Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) is that 'armalah' is a mature woman who had a husband who divorced her or died, whether consummated or not, and his statement is authoritative in language. Some said: 'armalah' is a woman without a husband without the condition that she had a husband. This is in al-Kifayah. If they are a people who can be counted, the poor and rich enter, and it becomes ownership for them. If they are a people who cannot be counted, it is not ownership for them, rather intended is closeness (to God), which is in fulfilling need, so it is given to the poor among them: meaning the poor of the orphans of the sons of Zayd, or the poor of their blind, and similarly for the rest. [1] His statement: 'a people who can be counted'; its interpretation according to Abu Yusuf is that they can be counted without book and calculation; Muhammad said: if they are more than a hundred, they cannot be counted; some said: it is delegated to the judge's opinion, and the fatwa is upon that, and this is what Muhammad's statement indicates. This is in al-Kifayah quoting from Fatawa Qadhi Khan.
Source pages p. 623

Commentary
This passage from the sharh explains the matn's ruling on bequests to specific categories of people: orphans, blind, crippled, and widows of a certain lineage. The key distinction is whether the group is countable ('ahsaw). If they are countable, the bequest is distributed to all members regardless of wealth or gender. If not countable, only the poor receive because the purpose of the bequest is charitable (qurbah). The commentary then defines the terms 'yatim' (orphan) and 'armalah' (widow/poor person) in detail. For 'yatim', it cites a hadith that orphanhood ends at puberty. For 'armalah', there is a discussion on whether it requires poverty or just lack of a husband, with the relied view being that it is a poor person without a spouse. Finally, it discusses the interpretation of 'ahsaw': the fatwa is to delegate to the judge's discretion.

Key Terms
يتيم (yatim): A minor whose father has died; orphan status ends at puberty.
أرمل (armal): A poor person unable to support themselves; for women, specifically a widow or divorced woman.
قوما يحصون (qawman yuhsawn): A group that can be counted precisely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a bequest to 'orphans of Banu Zayd', if the orphans are more than 100 in number, how should the bequest be distributed according to the relied-upon view?
A) Equally among all orphans
B) Only to the poor orphans
C) Only to the male orphans
D) To the judge's discretion

Key Takeaway
The distribution of a bequest to a group hinges on their enumerability: if countable, all share; if not, only the poor receive.

Bequest to 'Bani Fulan': Females Included
Ruling that females are included in a bequest to 'sons of' someone

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on including females in a bequest to 'bani fulan' and explain the linguistic basis.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, 'bani' in bequests includes both males and females.

This lesson addresses a common ambiguity in bequests: when someone leaves property 'to the sons of so-and-so', does it include daughters? The Hanafi school rules that it does, based on the general usage of the term 'bani'. Understanding this ruling ensures that bequests are implemented fairly and in line with the testator's probable intent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term
Recognize that 'bani fulan' (بني فلان) is the phrase used in the bequest. Determine its literal meaning and legal connotation.
Literal: 'sons of so-and-so'. Legal: often includes both sons and daughters.
2
Understand the Hanafi interpretation
The Hanafi school holds that 'bani' in this context includes male and female descendants. This is based on Arabic usage and authoritative texts.
The source cites Sunan Abi Dawud and al-Kifayah for support.
3
Apply to real scenarios
When a testator uses 'bani' in a bequest without explicit exclusion, the bequest is divided equally among all children (sons and daughters) unless the testator specifies otherwise.
If the testator says 'the males of my children', then only sons are included.

This short ruling states that when a bequest is made to 'the sons' (bani) of a person, the term includes both male and female offspring. The commentator explains that the word 'bani' in Arabic can include both genders when used in a general sense. The footnotes reference hadith and the work al-Kifayah for further evidence.

Key Terms
بني فلان (bani fulan): Literally 'sons of so-and-so', used in bequests to mean descendants or children, including both males and females.

In Hanafi law, a bequest to 'bani fulan' includes both male and female descendants unless explicitly excluded.

Invalidity of a Will to Mawali due to Ambiguity
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the invalidity of a will to 'mawali'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a will to 'mawali' is invalid in the Hanafi school and state the final position regarding inclusion of females.

Core Idea
A will to 'mawali' is invalid because 'mawla' is ambiguous; the final Hanafi position is that it includes only males.

In this passage, the commentator expands on a ruling from the base text: a bequest to one's 'mawali' is invalid because the term 'mawla' is shared between the manumitter and the manumitted, with no contextual clue to specify. The discussion then turns to whether females are included if the testator intended the manumitted. This intra-Hanafi dispute reveals how the school resolved the ambiguity through linguistic analysis and Imam Abu Hanifa's retraction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the ruling: a will to 'mawali' is invalid (batil). Note the reason: the word 'mawla' is mushtarak (shared/ambiguous).
The passage begins by affirming this ruling before discussing exceptions.
2
Understand the linguistic problem
Recognize that 'mawla' can mean the manumitter (mu'tiq) or the manumitted (mu'taq). Since it is shared, it has no general (amm) meaning, and without a qarina (indication), the will is void due to ignorance of the intended beneficiary.
This is a foundational Hanafi principle: a mushtarak cannot be used in a general sense without specification.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute on females
Note the two positions: (1) Abu Hanifa's first view and both Sahibayn: females are included when mixed with males because the plural of males includes females. (2) Abu Hanifa's later view: only males are included because the literal meaning of the noun applies to males. Understand the reasoning for the retraction (al-ruju').
The commentator contrasts this with tribal names (Banu so-and-so) which include both due to lineage, not literal personhood.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i view
The Shafi'i school holds the will is valid for all because each individual is called 'mawla', analogous to 'brothers'. Contrast this with the Hanafi emphasis on ambiguity.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The final relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's later view: only males are included when the testator intends the manumitted. This is recorded in al-Hidayah.
This illustrates how later Hanafi practice follows the Imam's final ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبطلت الوصية لمواليه فيمن معتقون ومعتقون) ؛ لأنَّ اللفظ مشترك ، ولا عموم له، ولا قرينة تدلُّ على أحدهما، وفي بعض كتب الشَّافِعِي أن الوصية للكل. في «الكفاية» : إنّما يدخلُ الإناث تحت هذه الوصيّة فيما إذا اختلط الذكور والإناث ، وأما إذا انفرد الإناث فلا شيء لهن بالاتفاق. وهذا أي دخولُ الإناث في هذه الوصيّة أوّل قول أبي حنيفة له، وهو قولهما ؛ لأنَّ جمع الذكور يتناول الإناث، ثم رجع وقال : يتناول الذكور خاصة ؛ لأنَّ حقيقة الاسم المذكور وانتظامه للإناث يجوز ، والكلام حقيقة بخلاف ما إذا كان بنو فلان اسم قبيلة أو فخذ، حيث يتناول الذكور والإناث ؛ لأنه ليس يراد بها أعيانهم ؛ إذ هو مجرد الانتساب. كذا في «الهداية». قوله : لأن اللفظ مشترك... الخ ؛ توضيحه : إن الجهة مختلفة ؛ لأنّ أحدهما : أي المعتق بكسر التاء يسمى مولى النعمة، والثاني : أي المعتق بفتح التاء يسمى منعم عليه، فصار لفظ المولى مشتركاً، ولا عموم له ؛ أي لا يجوز عموم المشترك، بأن يراد به كلا معنييه معاً بإطلاق واحد، ولا قرينة تدل على أحد : أي لا ترجيح البعض على البعض فبقي مجهول المراد، فلا يدرى أنّ المراد أيهما. قوله : للكل ؛ لأن الاسم يتناولهم ؛ لأن كلاً منهم يسمّى مولى، فصار كالأخوة. كذا في «الهداية».
And the will to his mawali is invalid for both manumitted persons and manumittors, because the word is shared (ambiguous), and it has no general meaning, and there is no indication towards one of the two. And in some books of al-Shafi'i that the will is for all. In al-Kifaya: Females enter under this will only when males and females are mixed; but if females are alone, they get nothing by consensus. And this (i.e., the inclusion of females in this will) is the first saying of Abu Hanifa, and it is the saying of both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), because the plural of males includes females. Then he retracted and said: it refers specifically to males, because the reality of the mentioned noun and its arrangement for females is permissible, and speech is literal, unlike when 'banu so-and-so' is a name of a tribe or clan, where it includes males and females because it does not intend their persons, since it is mere lineage. Thus in al-Hidaya. His saying: 'Because the word is shared...' etc.; its clarification: The aspect is different because one (i.e., the manumitted person, with kasra on the ta) is called a mawla of grace, and the second (i.e., the manumittor, with fatha on the ta) is called the one upon whom grace is conferred. Thus the word mawla becomes shared, and it has no general meaning, i.e., the general meaning of a shared term is not permitted, such that both meanings are intended together with a single utterance, and there is no indication towards one, i.e., no preference of some over others, so the intended meaning remains unknown, and it is not known which is meant. His saying: 'For all' because the noun includes them, since each of them is called mawla, so it becomes like brothers. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 625

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute, expanded from the base matn. The ruling is that a will to 'mawali' is invalid because the word 'mawla' is ambiguous (mushtarak) between the manumitter (mu'tiq) and the manumitted (mu'taq). Since there is no general meaning for a shared term and no contextual indication (qarina) to specify one meaning, the will is invalid due to ignorance of the intended beneficiary. The commentator then discusses the inclusion of females: the initial Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa's first view, also held by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) was that 'mawali' includes females when mixed with males, because the Arabic plural of males can include females. However, Abu Hanifa later retracted and held that it refers only to males, because the literal meaning of the noun applies only to males; if the term were a tribal name (like 'Banu so-and-so') it would include both, but here it denotes actual persons. The commentator also notes the Shafi'i view that the will is valid for all, as each individual is called a mawla, analogous to 'brothers'.

Key Terms
مولى (mawla): In this passage, mawla is ambiguous, referring either to the manumitter (mu'tiq) or the manumitted (mu'taq).
الوصية (al-wasiyya): A bequest or will, valid up to one-third of the estate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the final Hanafi position, whom does the word 'mawali' in a will include?
A) Only the manumitters
B) Only the manumitted males
C) Both males and females among the manumitted
D) All manumitters and manumitted

Key Takeaway
A will to 'mawali' is invalid due to linguistic ambiguity; the relied-upon Hanafi position is that it only includes males if clarified.

Bequest of Usufruct: Service and Residence
Ruling on the validity of bequest of usufruct with qiyas proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on bequest of usufruct and apply the qiyas argument for its validity, including the one-third limit.

Core Idea
A bequest of a slave's service or a house's residence is valid because usufruct can be owned separately, just like during life.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains that bequests of usufructs (manafi') such as service and residence are valid in the Hanafi school. The proof is qiyas: if one can transfer usufruct during life for compensation (ijara) or without (ariyah), then the same applies after death due to need. We will walk through the qiyas structure and the practical limitations of the one-third rule.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify the explicit ruling: the bequest of a slave's service, a house's residence, and their produce (ghalla) is valid, whether for a specified period or forever.
This is the core legal statement of the passage.
2
Identify the asl (original case)
The original case is the transfer of usufruct during life through lease (ijara) for compensation or loan (ariyah) without compensation. These are established as valid.
The asl provides the basis for qiyas.
3
Apply qiyas
The illa (effective cause) is the need for usufruct and the ability to separate ownership of usufruct from the corpus. Since this need exists both in life and after death, the ruling transfers to bequests.
Qiyas is jali (clear) because the illa is evident.
4
Note the scope
The bequest of usufruct is valid for a limited period or perpetually, analogous to ariyah (loan), which is a transfer of ownership according to the Hanafi school.
This distinguishes it from inheritance, which is not a transfer of ownership.
5
Apply the one-third limit
If the value of the corpus (slave or house) does not exceed one-third of the estate, it is handed over fully. If it exceeds, the house is divided into thirds (one-third to the legatee) and the slave is shared by turns (muhaya'ah).
The one-third limit applies to the corpus, not just the usufruct value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الوصية بالسكنى والخدمة. تصح الوصية بخدمة عبده، وسكنى داره مُدَّةً معينة وأبداً وبغلتهما، فإن خرجت الرقبة من الثلث سُلّمت إليه لها، وإلا قسم الدار أثلاثاً ويُهايا العبد. قوله : باب الوصية بالسكنى والخدمة؛ لما فرغ من أحكام الوصايا المتعلقة بالأعيان شرع في بيان أحكام الوصايا المتعلقة بالمنافع، وأخَّرَ هذا الباب لما أن المنافع بعد الأعيان وجوداً، فأخرها عنها وضعاً. كذا في «العناية». قوله : تصح الوصية... الخ؛ لأن المنافع يصح تمليكها في حالة الحياة ببدل، كما في الإجارة، وبغير بدل كما في العارية، فكذا بعد الممات؛ لحاجته كما في الأعيان. ويكون محبوساً على ملك الموصي في حق المنفعة، حتى يتملكها الموصى له على ملكه، كما يستوفي الموقوف عليه منافع الوقف على حكم ملك الواقف، وتصح مؤقتاً ومؤيداً كما في العارية، فإنّها تمليك على أصلنا بخلاف الميراث. قوله : وبغلتهما؛ أي تصح الوصيّةُ بغلّة العبدِ والدار؛ لأنه بدل المنفعة، فأخذ حكمها، والمعنى يشملهما الغلة: كلّ ما يحصل من ربع أرض أو كرائهما، أو أجرة غلام أو نحو ذلك. كذا في «المغرب». قوله : فإن خرجت... الخ؛ أي إن خرجت رقبةُ العبد من الثلث سُلِّمت إلى الموصى لـه؛ لأجل الوصية، يعني ليخدم العبد الموصى به للموصى له؛ لأنَّ حق الموصى له في الثلث لا تزاحمه الورثة. كذا في «الهداية». قوله : وإلا... الخ؛ أي وإن لم تخرج الرقبة يعني وإن كان لا مال للموصي
Chapter of bequest of residence and service. The bequest of the service of his slave and the residence of his house for a specified period or forever and of their produce is valid. If the corpus (slave or house) comes out of the one-third (of the estate), it is handed over to the legatee; otherwise, the house is divided into thirds and the slave is shared by turns. His saying: 'Chapter of bequest of residence and service'; since he finished the rulings on bequests related to corporeal things, he began to explain the rulings on bequests related to usufructs, and he delayed this chapter because usufructs exist after corporeal things, so he placed them after them. Thus in al-`Inayah. His saying: 'The bequest is valid...' etc.; because it is valid to transfer ownership of usufructs during life for compensation, as in lease, and without compensation, as in loan, so too after death due to need, as with corporeal things. And it remains attached to the ownership of the testator with respect to the right of usufruct, until the legatee owns it on his own ownership, just as the beneficiary of a waqf receives the benefits of the waqf subject to the ownership of the waqif. And it is valid for a limited period or perpetually, as in a loan, for it is a transfer of ownership according to our school, unlike inheritance. His saying: 'And their produce'; i.e., the bequest of the produce of the slave and the house is valid because it is the substitute for usufruct, so it takes its ruling, and the meaning includes both: all that is obtained from the rent of land or its hire, or the wages of a servant or the like. Thus in al-Mughrib. His saying: 'If the corpus comes out...'; i.e., if the corpus of the slave comes out of the one-third, it is handed over to the legatee for the sake of the bequest, meaning that the slave serves the legatee, because the legatee's right is in the one-third and the heirs do not compete with him. Thus in al-Hidayah. His saying: 'Otherwise...'; i.e., if the corpus does not come out, meaning if the testator has no property...
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, expanded from the base matn. It establishes that a bequest of usufruct (such as the service of a slave or residence in a house) is valid, whether for a limited period or forever, and also the bequest of their produce (like rent). The proof is qiyas (analogy): just as usufruct can be transferred in life for compensation (ijara) or without (ariyah), so too after death due to need. The usufruct remains attached to the testator's ownership until the legatee takes possession, similar to waqf. The passage then discusses the one-third limit: if the value of the slave or house is within one-third of the estate, it is handed over fully; otherwise, the house is divided into three parts (one-third to the legatee) and the slave is shared by turns (muhaya'ah). The commentary clarifies that the produce (ghalla) includes rent or wages, as it is a substitute for usufruct.

Key Terms
السكنى (al-sukna): The right to reside in a house; in this passage, it is the object of a bequest, valid for a specified period or forever.
الخدمة (al-khidma): The service of a slave; in this passage, it is the object of a bequest, valid as a usufruct.
الغلة (al-ghalla): The produce or income from a property, such as rent from land or wages of a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The entire house is given to the legatee
B) The house is divided into three parts, one-third to the legatee
C) The house is sold and the proceeds divided
D) The bequest is entirely invalid

Key Takeaway
A bequest of usufruct is valid in the Hanafi school, subject to the one-third limit, and is proven by analogy to lifetime transfers.

Bequest of Usufruct: Validity and Division Rules
Explains validity and division rules for bequest of usufruct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bequest of usufruct and apply the division rules (tertial division and muhaya'ah) when the asset exceeds the one-third limit.

Core Idea
Bequest of usufruct is valid by qiyas; when principal exceeds one-third, division is by thirds or time-sharing.

This lesson explains the Hanafi ruling on bequests of usufruct—service of a slave, dwelling in a house, and their produce. Unlike some other schools, the Hanafi school validates such bequests by analogy to contracts during life (ijarah and ariyah). The passage also details how to divide the usufruct when the bequeathed asset's principal exceeds the allowable one-third of the estate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage begins by stating that a bequest of service or dwelling is valid. Identify that the hukm is sahih (valid) and note that it applies to a specified period or forever, and includes produce (ghallah).
The ruling covers both temporary and permanent bequests of usufruct.
2
Trace the proof (qiyas)
The text uses qiyas: just as usufruct can be transferred during life (for consideration in ijarah, gratuitously in ariyah), so too after death due to need. Recognize this as qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
The qiyas is based on the permissibility of transferring usufruct independently of the principal.
3
Analyze the condition: principal within or beyond the third
When the bequeathed asset's principal (value) is within one-third of the estate, it is given entirely to the legatee. Otherwise, the house is divided into thirds (physical division for real estate) and the slave (indivisible) is shared by time (muhaya'ah).
The legatee's right is limited to one-third of the asset's usufruct, not the asset itself.
4
Compare with bequest of tangible assets
The text notes that the rule is similar to bequest of ayn (tangible property), establishing consistency in the law of wills.
The underlying principle is that the legatee's share is limited to one-third of the estate.
5
Note cross-madhhab disagreement
The Shafi'i school holds such bequests invalid because usufruct is not a tangible asset; Hanafi responds by qiyas on ijarah and ariyah.
This difference is a legitimate ikhtilaf based on different usul principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الوصية بالسكنى والخدمة

تصح الوصية بخدمة عبده، وسكنى داره مدة معينة وأبداً وبغلتهما، فإن خرجت الرقبة من الثلث سلمت إليه، وإلا قسم الدار أثلاثاً ويُهايا العبد.

قوله: باب الوصية بالسكنى والخدمة؛ لما فرغ من أحكام الوصايا المتعلقة بالأعيان شرع في بيان أحكام الوصايا المتعلقة بالمنافع، وأخر هذا الباب لما أن المنافع بعد الأعيان وجوداً، فأخرها عنها وضعاً. كذا في «العناية».

قوله: تصح الوصية... الخ؛ لأن المنافع يصح تمليكها في حالة الحياة ببدل، كما في الإجارة، وبغير بدل كما في العارية، فكذا بعد الممات؛ لحاجته كما في الأعيان. ويكون محبوساً على ملك الموصي في حق المنفعة، حتى يتملكها الموصى له على ملكه، كما يستوفي الموقوف عليه منافع الوقف على حكم ملك الواقف، وتصح مؤقتاً ومؤيداً كما في العارية، فإنها تمليك على أصلنا بخلاف الميراث.

قوله: وبغلتهما؛ أي تصح الوصية بغلة العبد والدار؛ لأنه بدل المنفعة، فأخذ حكمها، والمعنى يشملهما الغلة: كل ما يحصل من ربع أرض أو كرائهما، أو أجرة غلام أو نحو ذلك. كذا في «المغرب».

قوله: فإن خرجت... الخ؛ أي إن خرجت رقبة العبد من الثلث سلمت إلى الموصى له؛ لأجل الوصية، يعني ليخدم العبد الموصى به للموصى له؛ لأن حق الموصى له في الثلث لا تزاحمه الورثة. كذا في «الهداية».

قوله: وإلا... الخ؛ أي وإن لم تخرج الرقبة، يعني وإن كان لا مال للموصي غيره قسم الدار، ويسلم إلى الموصى له مقدار ثلث المال؛ لأن حقه في الثلث، وحقهم في الثلثين، كما في الوصية في العين ولا تمكن قسمة العبد أجزاء؛ لأنه لا يتجزأ، فصرنا إلى المهايأة إيفاء للحقين. كذا في «الهداية».
Chapter of bequest of dwelling and service. The bequest of the service of his slave, and the dwelling in his house for a specified period or forever, and of their produce is valid. Then if the principal (body) comes out of the one-third (i.e., is within the third), it is handed over to him (the legatee). Otherwise, the house is divided into thirds, and the slave is made subject to time-sharing (muhaya'ah).
Source pages p. 626

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn. The chapter addresses bequests of usufruct (manafi') after completing bequests of tangible assets (a'yan). The commentary explains that bequests of usufruct are valid by analogy (qiyas) to contracts during life: just as one can transfer usufruct for a consideration (ijarah) or gratuitously (ariyah) during life, so too after death due to need. The usufruct is considered attached to the testator's ownership until the legatee takes possession, akin to a waqf. The bequest of 'ghallah' (produce, such as rent or wages) is also valid as it is the substitute for usufruct. When the bequeathed asset's principal exceeds one-third of the estate, the ruling depends: if the principal is within the one-third (i.e., its value is not more than one-third of the total estate), it is given entirely to the legatee; otherwise, if the testator has no other property, the house is divided physically into thirds (the legatee gets one-third of the house to dwell in), and the slave, being indivisible, is shared by time (muhaya'ah) — the legatee uses his service for a portion of time proportionate to his share (one-third), and the heirs for the remaining two-thirds. This is based on the principle that the legatee's right is in one-third and the heirs' in two-thirds, similar to bequest of a tangible asset (ayn).

Key Terms
رقبة (raqabah): The principal or body of the property (slave or house) as distinct from its usufruct.
منفعة (manfa'ah): Usufruct; the right to use or benefit from the property (e.g., service of a slave, dwelling in a house).
مهايأة (muhāyā'ah): Time-sharing arrangement for an indivisible asset (like a slave) to give each party proportional use.
غلة (ghallah): Produce or income generated by property, such as rent, wages, crop yields.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if a testator bequeaths the services of a slave but the slave's value exceeds one-third of the estate?
A) The bequest is void.
B) The slave is sold and the proceeds are divided between legatee and heirs.
C) The slave is shared by time between the legatee and the heirs.
D) The legatee gets the slave for free because the bequest is valid entirely.

Key Takeaway
Bequest of usufruct is valid in Hanafi law; when the principal exceeds one-third, division is by thirds (divisible assets) or time-sharing (indivisible assets).

Legatee's Death and Bequest of Fruit vs. Produce
Two rulings: legatee's death and fruit vs. produce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the two rulings: (1) state when a bequest becomes void or returns to heirs based on the timing of the legatee's death, and (2) differentiate between 'fruit' (thamarah) and 'produce' (ghallah) in a bequest to determine what the legatee receives.

Core Idea
A bequest is void if the legatee dies before the testator; fruit includes only existing fruit unless 'ever' is added—produce covers present and future.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah presents two distinct but closely related rulings in the law of bequests (wasiyyah). The first ruling addresses the critical timing of the legatee's death relative to the testator's death, determining whether the bequest is void or reverts to the testator's heirs. The second ruling explores the scope of a bequest of an orchard's fruit or produce, hinging on a subtle but crucial distinction in Hanafi custom between 'thamar' (fruit) and 'ghallah' (produce/income). Understanding these rulings is essential for correctly interpreting and executing bequests in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage contains two separate rulings: first, the effect of the legatee's death (whether before or after the testator) on the bequest; second, the scope of a bequest of fruit (thamar) vs. produce (ghallah). Recognize that the second ruling has two sub-cases: when 'ever' (abad) is included or omitted.
The commentary uses the phrase 'qawluh' to introduce each ruling, so the division is explicit.
2
Analyze the death-timing ruling
Understand the principle: a bequest becomes effective only after the testator's death, so the legatee must be alive at that moment. If the legatee dies before the testator, the offer (ijaab) cannot be accepted, so the bequest is void. If the legatee dies after the testator, the bequest was already effective, but upon the legatee's death the usufruct returns to the testator's heirs because the legatee only had a personal right.
The analogy with a seller making an offer after death helps clarify why the legatee must survive.
3
Analyze the fruit vs. produce distinction
The second ruling: when a testator bequeaths 'the fruit of my orchard', only the fruit existing at the time of death belongs to the legatee. Future fruit is included only if the testator explicitly says 'ever' (abad). However, if the bequest is of 'produce' (ghallah), then even without 'ever' it covers both present and future produce, because customarily 'ghallah' includes periodic income.
The key difference is linguistic custom: 'thamar' refers only to what exists, 'ghallah' includes what will arise.
4
Note the role of custom ('urf)
The distinction between thamar and ghallah is based on 'urf (customary usage). Hanafi law gives weight to 'urf in interpretation, so the same wording may differ in scope depending on what is customary. This ruling illustrates how 'urf shapes legal outcomes.
Other schools may not make this distinction, so cross-madhhab differences arise.
5
Apply the rulings to scenarios
Now apply the rules: given a scenario, determine whether the bequest is valid/void or what the legatee receives. For example, if T bequeaths 'the fruit of my orchard' to L, and T dies with fruit, and L dies after T, L's heirs get only the fruit at T's death. If T had said 'the fruit of my orchard ever', L gets future fruit too. If T bequeaths 'the produce of my orchard', L gets all future produce automatically.
Practice with different combinations of death timing and wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبموته في حياة موصيه تبطل، وبعد موته يعود إلى الورثة، وبثمرة بستانه إن مات وفيه ثمرة له هذه فقط، وإن ضم أبداً، فله هذه وما يحدث كما في غلة بستانه.

قوله: وبموته... الخ؛ أي موت الموصى له في حياة موصيه تبطل الوصية؛ لأن إيجاب الوصية يكون بعد الموت؛ لأنه متعلق بوجود الموت، فإذا مات الموصى له لم يصح الإيجاب، كما لا يصح إيجاب البائع للمشتري بعد موته. كذا في «غاية البيان».

قوله: وبعد موته يعود إلى الورثة؛ أي بموت الموصى له بعد موت موصيه تعود إلى ورثة الموصي؛ لأنه أوصى بأن ينتفع الموصى له على ملك الموصي، فإذا مات الموصى له يعود إلى ورثة الموصي بحكم الملك.

قوله: وبثمرة بستانه إن مات وفيه ثمرة له هذه فقط؛ أي للموصى له الثمرة الكائنة حال موت الموصي لا ما يحدث بعده، وإن ضم أبداً، فله هذه وما يحدث كما في غلة بستانه؛ أي أوصى بغلة بستانه سواء ضم لفظ الأبد أو لا، فله هذه وما يحدث.

قوله: سواء ضم... الخ؛ والفرق أن الثمرة اسم للموجود عرفاً فلا يتناول المعدوم إلا بدلالة زائدة مثل التنصيص على الأبد، أما الغلة فتنتظم الموجود، وما يكون بعرض الوجود مرة بعد أخرى عرفاً، فإذا أطلقت يتناولهما عرفاً غير موقوف على دلالة أخرى بخلاف الثمرة؛ فلهذا يفتقر الانصراف في الثمرة إلى دليل زائد. هذا خلاصة ما في «الهداية».
And by his (the legatee's) death during the life of his testator, it (the bequest) becomes void, and after his death it returns to the heirs. And (as for) the fruit of his orchard, if he dies and there is fruit in it, he (the legatee) has only this (existing fruit), but if he includes the word 'ever', then he has this and what arises later, as in the produce of his orchard.
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings from the matn with commentary. First ruling: If the legatee dies before the testator, the bequest becomes void because a bequest is effective only after the testator's death; it is contingent upon the testator's death occurring while the legatee is alive. The analogy given is that a seller cannot make an offer of sale after his death, similarly the testator's offer (bequest) cannot be accepted after the legatee's prior death. If the legatee dies after the testator, the usufruct returns to the testator's heirs because the legatee's right was only a personal right of enjoyment on the testator's property, and upon his death, it reverts to the heirs by virtue of their ownership. Second ruling: Regarding a bequest of the fruit (thamar) of an orchard, the legatee only gets the fruit that exists at the time of the testator's death. If the testator adds the word 'ever' (abad), then the legatee gets both existing and future fruit, similar to a bequest of 'ghallah' (produce/income). The key difference: 'thamar' in custom refers only to what is currently present, so it does not include future fruit unless there is an explicit indication (like 'ever'). In contrast, 'ghallah' customarily includes both what exists and what will come into existence periodically, so it does not require additional wording to include future produce.

Key Terms
ثمرة (thamarah): Fruit of trees; in bequest context, it refers only to existing fruit at the time of testator's death unless the word 'ever' is added.
غلة (ghallah): Produce/income (e.g., rent, crop yield, wages); includes both what exists and what arises periodically by custom.
وصية (wasiyyah): A bequest or will made by a testator, taking effect after death, subject to Hanafi conditions.
أبد (abad): Literally 'ever/forever'; when added to a bequest of fruit, it extends the bequest to future fruit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A testator bequeaths 'the fruit of my orchard' without adding 'ever'. The testator dies when there is fruit. The legatee dies after the testator but before harvesting the fruit. Who gets the fruit?
A) The legatee's heirs get the fruit.
B) The fruit is lost because the legatee did not harvest.
C) The testator's heirs get the fruit.
D) The legatee's heirs get the fruit and all future fruit.

Key Takeaway
A bequest is void if the legatee predeceases the testator; the scope of a bequest of 'fruit' is limited to existing produce unless 'ever' is added, while 'produce' automatically covers both present and future yields.

Death of Legatee: When the Bequest Becomes Void or Returns to Heirs
Explains death of legatee and its effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the legatee dies before vs after the testator, and apply the qiyas to sale to justify the consequence.

Core Idea
A bequest is void if the legatee dies before the testator; if after, it returns to the testator's heirs.

The validity of a bequest hinges on the legatee being alive at the testator's death. This lesson unpacks the two key scenarios from the commentary: death before the testator voids the bequest, and death after causes it to revert to the testator's heirs. The reasoning is drawn from an analogy to a sale contract, where an offer cannot be made after the offeror's death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text distinguishes between the legatee dying (1) during the testator's lifetime and (2) after the testator's death.
The key temporal point is when the bequest takes effect.
2
Determine the ruling for each
If the legatee dies before the testator, the bequest becomes void. If the legatee dies after the testator, the bequest returns to the testator's heirs.
Void means the property remains with the testator's estate; return means it goes to the heirs.
3
Understand the rationale
The bequest is a transfer of ownership that takes effect after the testator's death. Therefore, the legatee must be alive at that moment for the transfer to occur.
This is rooted in the nature of wasiyyah as a deferred contract.
4
Analyze the qiyas (analogy) to sale
Just as a seller cannot make an offer after his death, a testator cannot effectively bequeath to a legatee who dies before him, because the creation of the bequest occurs at the testator's death.
The analogy is explicit: 'as the seller's offer after his death is not valid'.
5
Apply to a concrete case
If the testator dies, then the legatee dies a week later, the bequest does not become void; instead, the bequeathed property reverts to the testator's heirs because the legatee's right terminated
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: practical consequence.

And with his death during the life of his testator, it becomes void, and after his death it returns to the heirs. And regarding the fruit of his orchard: if he dies and there is fruit in it, he has only that. And if he binds it forever, he has this and what will appear, as in the usufruct of his orchard. One third of the property for him to reside in, and the slave serves the legatee to the extent that the bequest is valid, and serves the heirs to the extent that it is not valid. 'And with his death during the life of his testator, it becomes void, and after his death it returns to the heirs': that is, with the death of the legatee after the death of his testator, it returns to the heirs of the testator, because he bequeathed that the legatee benefit from the testator's property, so when the legatee dies, it returns to the testator's heirs by rule. 'And regarding the fruit of his orchard: if he dies and there is fruit in it, he has only that': that is, the legatee has the fruit existing at the time of the testator's death, not what appears after. 'And if he binds it forever, he has this and what will appear, as in the usufruct of his orchard': that is, he bequeathed the usufruct of his orchard, whether he included the term 'forever' or not, so he has this and what will appear. [2] The property. Other than that, he divides the house and hands over to the legatee the amount of one third of the property, because his right is in the third, and their right is in the two-thirds, as in the bequest of a specific thing, and it is not possible to divide the slave into parts because he is indivisible, so we resort to temporal division to fulfill both rights. As in al-Hidaya. [1] His saying: 'And with his death...' etc.: that is, the death of the legatee during the life of his testator voids the bequest, because the creation of the bequest is after death, since it is attached to the occurrence of death. So if the legatee dies, the creation is not valid, just as the creation of the seller for the buyer after his death is not valid. As in Ghayat al-Bayan. [2] His saying: 'whether he included...' etc.: the difference is that 'fruit' is a name for what exists customarily, so it does not include non-existent except with extra indication such as specifying 'forever'. As for 'usufruct', it encompasses the existing and what is on the verge of existence time after time customarily, so if it is unrestricted, it covers them customarily without being dependent on another indication, unlike fruit. Hence the reference in fruit needs extra evidence. This is the summary of what is in al-Hidayah (4:252) and (4:255).
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from al-Wiqaya. It explains several interrelated rulings in the book of bequests (Kitab al-Wasaya). First: if the legatee dies before the testator, the bequest is void because the bequest is a transfer of ownership that takes effect after the testator's death; if the legatee is not alive at that time, the transfer cannot occur. This is likened to a sale contract where the seller's offer cannot be effective after the seller's death. Second: if the legatee dies after the testator, the bequest returns to the testator's heirs since the legatee's right was merely a right to benefit from the testator's property, and upon his death that right terminates. Third: regarding a bequest of fruit from an orchard, only the fruit existing at the testator's death belongs to the legatee. If the testator explicitly says 'forever', then the legatee also gets future fruit, similar to a bequest of usufruct (ghallah) where future yields are included by default because 'usufruct' customarily implies recurring benefits. Fourth: if the bequest is of one third of a property (e.g., a house) to a legatee, the legatee takes one third, and if the property is indivisible (like a slave), they use temporal division (muhaya'ah) to share the benefit according to their shares. The commentary provides the rationale based on custom (urf) and analogy (qiyas).

Key Terms
الموصى له (al-mūṣā lah): The beneficiary of a bequest.
الموصي (al-mūṣī): The testator.
الوصية (al-waṣiyyah): A bequest or will, effective after death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The bequest is transferred to the legatee's heirs.

D) The bequest is valid and the legatee's heirs inherit it.

Key Takeaway
A bequest requires the legatee to be alive at the testator's death; otherwise it is void or returns to heirs.

Scope of Bequest: Fruit (Thamarah) vs Usufruct (Ghallah) and Division of Indivisible Property
Explains scope of bequest: fruit vs usufruct, and division of indivisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a bequest of fruit (thamarah) and usufruct (ghallah) in terms of inclusion of future yields, and explain how an indivisible bequest is divided through muhaya'ah.

Core Idea
Bequest of 'fruit' includes only existing fruit; bequest of 'usufruct' includes future yields by default; indivisible property is shared by temporal division.

This lesson explores the nuances of what is included in a bequest when the testator uses specific terms like 'fruit' (thamarah) versus 'usufruct' (ghallah). The Hanafi school relies on custom (urf) to interpret these terms. Additionally, when the bequeathed property is indivisible (e.g., a slave), the heirs and legatee share through temporal division (muhaya'ah) rather than physical partition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms: thamarah and ghallah
The text contrasts 'fruit of the orchard' (thamar) with 'usufruct of the orchard' (ghallah). The key is what each term includes by default.
Thamarah refers to the fruit itself; ghallah refers to the recurring produce or rent.
2
Determine the scope for thamarah
If the testator bequeaths 'the fruit' without specifying 'forever', only the fruit existing at the testator's death belongs to the legatee. Future fruit is not included.
Customarily, 'fruit' is the physically present fruit, not future ones.
3
Determine the scope for ghallah
If the testator bequeaths 'the usufruct', whether or not he says 'forever', the legatee gets both existing and future yields. This is because 'usufruct' customarily implies recurring benefits.
The commentary explains: 'ghallah includes what exists and what is on the verge of existence time after time'.
4
Understand the rationale based on custom
The difference is based on 'urf (custom): 'fruit' is a term for what is actually present, while 'usufruct' denotes a series of benefits that continue. Hence, future fruit requires an explicit extension like 'forever', whereas future usufruct is included without such specification.
This is a key application of 'urf in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Apply the division method for indivisible property
When a bequest involves an indivisible asset (e.g., a slave), physical division is impossible. Instead, the heirs and legatee enter into muhaya'ah (temporal division) where each takes turns using the property proportionate to their share.
Example: if the legatee is entitled to one-third, he uses the slave one-third of the time.

And with his death during the life of his testator, it becomes void, and after his death it returns to the heirs. And regarding the fruit of his orchard: if he dies and there is fruit in it, he has only that. And if he binds it forever, he has this and what will appear, as in the usufruct of his orchard. One third of the property for him to reside in, and the slave serves the legatee to the extent that the bequest is valid, and serves the heirs to the extent that it is not valid. 'And with his death during the life of his testator, it becomes void, and after his death it returns to the heirs': that is, with the death of the legatee after the death of his testator, it returns to the heirs of the testator, because he bequeathed that the legatee benefit from the testator's property, so when the legatee dies, it returns to the testator's heirs by rule. 'And regarding the fruit of his orchard: if he dies and there is fruit in it, he has only that': that is, the legatee has the fruit existing at the time of the testator's death, not what appears after. 'And if he binds it forever, he has this and what will appear, as in the usufruct of his orchard': that is, he bequeathed the usufruct of his orchard, whether he included the term 'forever' or not, so he has this and what will appear. [2] The property. Other than that, he divides the house and hands over to the legatee the amount of one third of the property, because his right is in the third, and their right is in the two-thirds, as in the bequest of a specific thing, and it is not possible to divide the slave into parts because he is indivisible, so we resort to temporal division to fulfill both rights. As in al-Hidaya. [1] His saying: 'And with his death...' etc.: that is, the death of the legatee during the life of his testator voids the bequest, because the creation of the bequest is after death, since it is attached to the occurrence of death. So if the legatee dies, the creation is not valid, just as the creation of the seller for the buyer after his death is not valid. As in Ghayat al-Bayan. [2] His saying: 'whether he included...' etc.: the difference is that 'fruit' is a name for what exists customarily, so it does not include non-existent except with extra indication such as specifying 'forever'. As for 'usufruct', it encompasses the existing and what is on the verge of existence time after time customarily, so if it is unrestricted, it covers them customarily without being dependent on another indication, unlike fruit. Hence the reference in fruit needs extra evidence. This is the summary of what is in al-Hidayah (4:252) and (4:255).
Source pages p. 627

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from al-Wiqaya. It explains several interrelated rulings in the book of bequests (Kitab al-Wasaya). First: if the legatee dies before the testator, the bequest is void because the bequest is a transfer of ownership that takes effect after the testator's death; if the legatee is not alive at that time, the transfer cannot occur. This is likened to a sale contract where the seller's offer cannot be effective after the seller's death. Second: if the legatee dies after the testator, the bequest returns to the testator's heirs since the legatee's right was merely a right to benefit from the testator's property, and upon his death that right terminates. Third: regarding a bequest of fruit from an orchard, only the fruit existing at the testator's death belongs to the legatee. If the testator explicitly says 'forever', then the legatee also gets future fruit, similar to a bequest of usufruct (ghallah) where future yields are included by default because 'usufruct' customarily implies recurring benefits. Fourth: if the bequest is of one third of a property (e.g., a house) to a legatee, the legatee takes one third, and if the property is indivisible (like a slave), they use temporal division (muhaya'ah) to share the benefit according to their shares. The commentary provides the rationale based on custom (urf) and analogy (qiyas).

Key Terms
الغلة (al-ghallah): Usufruct or produce that recurs periodically.
الثمرة (al-thamarah): Fruit physically present at a given time.
المهايأة (al-muhāya’ah): Temporal division of benefit from an indivisible asset.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for a bequest of fruit to include future fruit?
A) The testator must explicitly say 'forever'.
B) The fruit must be from a perennial tree.
C) The legatee must request it.
D) The bequest automatically includes future fruit.

Key Takeaway
Bequest terms are interpreted by custom: 'fruit' is limited to present fruit, while 'usufruct' includes future yields; indivisible assets share by temporal division.

Ruling on Wool, Offspring, and Milk in Wills
Explains that wool, offspring, and milk only include what exists at testator's death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on bequests of wool, offspring, and milk, explain why the addition of 'forever' does not extend to future produce, and apply the distinction based on custom and legal analogy.

Core Idea
In a bequest, wool, offspring, and milk only include what exists at death—'forever' cannot bring in non-existent items because Islamic law does not permit contracts for them.

This passage clarifies a subtle but crucial point in Hanafi succession law: when someone bequeaths the wool of their sheep, the offspring, or the milk, the beneficiary only receives what is actually present at the testator's death. Even adding the word 'forever' (أبداً) does not bring in future yields. The commentator explains this by distinguishing between different types of produce and using legal analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
State the core rule: a bequest of wool, offspring, or milk only includes what exists at the time of death, regardless of adding the word 'forever'.
2
Recognize the distinction between types of produce
Understand that 'usufruct' (ghallah) naturally includes both current and future yields by custom, while 'fruit' and 'wool' only refer to existing items.
3
Understand the role of custom (urf)
Custom (urf) defines the scope of terms like 'wool' and 'fruit'. This default interpretation is key to determining the extent of the bequest.
4
Analyze the legal analogy to contracts
The commentator uses qiyas: a contract for non-existent fruit is valid (e.g., musaqah), but a contract for non-existent wool is not. Therefore, adding 'forever' can extend to future fruit but not to future wool.
The effective cause (illa) is the legal validity of the underlying contract for non-existent items.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبصوف غنمه ، وولدها ، ولبنها له ما في وقتِ موتِهِ ضمّ أبدا أو لا، وبصوف غنمه وولدها، ولبنها له ما في وقتِ موته ضمّ أبداً أو لا) ، والفرق بين الثَّمرة والغلة والصوف : أنَّ الغلّةَ تطلقُ على الموجود، وعلى ما يوجد مرة بعد أخرى، والثّمرة والصُّوف لا يطلقان إلا على الموجود، إلا أنه إذا ضمّ أبداً صار قرينة دالة على تناول المعدوم، فتصح في الثَّمرة دون الصوف ؛ لأنَّ العقد على الثمرة المعدومة يصح شرعاً كالمساقاة لا على الصوف والولد ونحوهما.
And regarding the wool of his sheep, and its offspring, and its milk: he has what is at the time of his death, whether he included 'forever' or not. 'And regarding the wool of his sheep, its offspring, and its milk: he has what is at the time of his death, whether he included forever or not.' And the difference between fruit, usufruct, and wool is that 'usufruct' applies to the existing and to what comes into existence time after time, while 'fruit' and 'wool' apply only to the existing. However, if he includes 'forever', it becomes a context indicating inclusion of the non-existent, so it is valid for fruit but not for wool, because a contract for non-existent fruit is legally valid, like a musaqah contract, whereas it is not valid for wool, offspring, and the like.
Source pages p. 628

Commentary
This passage is a commentary distinguishing between different types of produce in a bequest. The base ruling states that a bequest of wool, offspring, or milk only includes what exists at the time of the testator's death, regardless of whether the word 'forever' is added. The commentary explains why this differs from fruit and usufruct. The key distinction is based on customary usage (urf) and legal validity: 'usufruct' (ghallah) is a term that customarily includes both current and future benefits, so adding 'forever' is unnecessary. 'Fruit' (thamarah) and 'wool' (suf) by custom only refer to what exists presently. However, adding 'forever' can extend 'fruit' to future fruit because a contract for future fruit (e.g., musaqah, sharecropping) is valid in Islamic law. In contrast, a contract for future wool, offspring, or milk (non-existent at contracting) is not valid in any recognized contract (e.g., sale, lease). Therefore, even with the word 'forever', a bequest of future wool, offspring, or milk is not valid because the underlying legal basis (the possibility of a valid contract) is absent. The commentary thus uses legal analogy and custom to reach this conclusion.

Key Terms
الصوف (al-ṣūf): Wool; in this context, existing wool from sheep at the time of the testator's death.
الغلة (al-ghallah): Usufruct or periodic produce (e.g., rent, fruit, crops) that includes future yields by default in custom.
المساقاة (al-musāqāh): A sharecropping contract for trees where one party provides care and the other provides trees; valid for future fruit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because a contract for non-existent wool is not valid in Islamic law, unlike fruit.
C) Because wool cannot be precisely measured.
D) Because the bequest exceeds one third of the estate.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a bequest of wool, offspring, or milk only covers what exists at death; 'forever' cannot bring in future produce because contracts for such non-existent items are invalid.

Inheritance of Churches and Validity of Wills for Places of Worship
Intra-Hanafi dispute on inheritance and wills for churches/synagogues

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's rulings on inheritance of churches built as waqf, and apply the analogous reasoning to wills for building places of worship.

Core Idea
A church made as waqf is inheritable under Abu Hanifa because ownership is not fully severed, unlike a mosque.

This passage presents an important intra-Hanafi dispute regarding non-Muslim places of worship. While mosques are considered fully dedicated to Allah and thus non-inheritable, churches and synagogues retain elements of ownership that allow inheritance according to Abu Hanifa. The same principle extends to wills: bequests to build such structures are valid. Understanding the legal reasoning behind these distinctions is key to mastering Hanafi fiqh on waqf and wasiyyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act in dispute
A non-Muslim makes a church or synagogue as a waqf during his health. Is this act valid? Can the building be inherited upon his death?
The dispute is about the nature of the act, not the status of the building afterwards.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling and its basis
Abu Hanifa rules the church is inheritable because he analogizes it to waqf, which he deems inheritable. He does not consider making a church a sin for non-Muslims.
For Abu Hanifa, waqf is a temporary endowment; ownership does not fully transfer to Allah.
3
State the Sahibayn's ruling and its basis
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule the act invalid because building a church is a sin (ma'siyah) in Islam, regardless of the testator's faith.
'Sin' here refers to the act of creating a place for false worship.
4
Analyze the key legal distinction: churches vs. mosques
The commentary explains that in a mosque, all ownership rights are severed; but in a church, owners retain rights of residence and burial, so ownership persists. Hence, a church is more like ordinary property.
This distinction is based on 'urf (custom) and actual practice.
5
Apply the same reasoning to wills (wasiyyah)
A will to build a church or synagogue is valid because wills have a broader scope than contracts. The bequest is for a permissible action from the testator's perspective.
The will is valid even if the beneficiary group is unnamed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٣] والوصية بجعل أحدهما سمي قوماً، أو لا تصح)، فإن أوصى يهودي أو نصراني أن يجعل القوم مسمين بيعة أو كنيسة تصح، ولقوم غير مسمين تصح [١] قوله: يصح شرعاً كالمساقاة؛ والإجارة والمعاملة، فإذا جازَ شرعاً بعقد من العقود المذكورة، جاز استحقاقها بالوصيّة أيضاً؛ لأنَّ باب الوصية أوسعُ من غيرها، وأما الصوف وأختاها فلا يصح استحقاقها أصلاً بعقد ما، فلم يصح استحقاقها بعقدِ الوصيّة. كذا في الجلبي» ناقلا عن «البيانية». (۲) (٤) قوله: تصح؛ وذلك لأنّ الوصية لقوم بأعيانهم تمليك، والذي يملك ذلك
And a church and synagogue made (as a waqf) during health are inherited, because this is like a waqf according to Abu Hanifa, and waqf is inheritable in his view. But according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): no, because it is a sin and thus invalid. [3] And a will to make one of them: if he names a people, it is valid, or if not named, it is valid. [1] His saying: 'valid legally like musaqah; and lease and transaction; so if it is legally permitted by any of the mentioned contracts, then entitlement to it by a will is also permitted because the chapter of wills is broader than others. As for wool and its sisters, entitlement to them is not valid at all by any contract, so their entitlement by a will is not valid.' This is from al-Jalabi, quoting al-Bayan. Source pages p. 628

Commentary
This passage addresses two scenarios involving non-Muslims (Jews and Christians) and places of worship. First: when a church or synagogue is made during the testator's health (i.e., as a charitable endowment - waqf), Abu Hanifa rules that it can be inherited by the testator's heirs because he analogizes it to a waqf, which is inheritable in his view. The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree, arguing that such a building is a sin and therefore the act is invalid, so it cannot be inherited. The commentary explains that churches are not like mosques because in a mosque, ownership is completely severed, but in a church, the owners retain the right to use and bury dead, so it is more like property that can be inherited. Second: regarding a will (wasiyyah) by a Jew or Christian to make a church or synagogue: if the testator specifies a particular group of people, the bequest is valid; even if unspecified, it is also valid. The commentary then contrasts this with wool and similar items, explaining that a contract for future wool is invalid, so a bequest of it is invalid, but here the bequest is for an existing building or to construct one, which is valid because wills are broader in scope.

Key Terms
بيعة (bī‘ah): A church; a place of worship for Christians.
كنيسة (kanīsah): A church or synagogue; a place of worship for Christians or Jews.
الوقف (al-waqf): Endowment; a charitable trust where ownership is transferred to Allah, but in Abu Hanifa's view, waqf is inheritable (the property remains with the heirs).
معصية (ma‘ṣiyah): A sin; in this context, building a church is considered an act of disobedience to Allah.

C) Because the testator was a non-Muslim, so the rules of waqf do not apply.
D) Because the Sahibayn's opinion is about wills, not waqf.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, churches and synagogues are treated as inheritable property when made as waqf, unlike mosques, and wills to build them are valid regardless of specification.

Validity of a Musta'min's Will: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on musta'min's will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on a musta'min's will, explain the legal reasoning (qurba vs. wasiyya bil-ma'siya), and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between villages and metropolises.

Core Idea
A musta'min's bequest for a church is piety in his belief, not sin — Abu Hanifa validates; Sahibayn disagree in villages, but all agree invalid in metropolises.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the will of a musta'min (a non-Muslim with safe conduct) who has no heirs in Muslim territory. The disagreement turns on whether bequeathing wealth for building a church is a valid act of piety (qurba) or a sinful bequest (wasiyya bil-ma'siya). The commentary also clarifies that this dispute is confined to rural areas, while in metropolises there is consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputants
We have a musta'min bequeathing all his wealth to a Muslim or dhimmi. Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ on validity.
Note: The musta'min is a non-Muslim granted safe conduct (aman) in Muslim territory.
2
State each side's ruling and proof
Abu Hanifa: valid because the bequest is considered qurba (piety) in the musta'min's belief. Sahibayn: invalid because it is a wasiyya bil-ma'siya (bequest for sin) since building a church is sinful in Islam.
Each side's proof is based on the nature of the act from the perspective of Islamic law.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa argues that the act is not sinful for the non-Muslim; it is a religious duty in their belief. Muslims leave them to their religion. Additionally, the musta'min's heirs in dar al-harb are legally considered dead, so no heir right is infringed.
This reasoning respects the non-Muslim's own standards of piety.
4
Note the geographical limitation
The dispute is only in villages (qura). In metropolises (amsar), all imams agree the will is invalid because non-Muslims are prohibited from building new churches there.
The geographical distinction comes from al-Kifaya and is crucial for correct fatwa.
5
Understand the condition 'no heir here'
If the musta'min had an heir in Muslim land, the will would be limited to one-third. This implication is considered weak by some scholars, but the matn (core text) takes precedence over commentaries.
The phrase 'no heir here' implies that if an heir were present, the will would be restricted, but this is not universally accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كوصية مستأمن لا وارث له هنا بكل ماله لمسلم أو ذمي. عند أبي حنيفة له لا عندهما ، فإن الوصية بالمعصية لا تصح ، له : أنه قربة في معتقدهم، وهم متروكون على ما يدينون. (كوصية مستأمن لا وارث له هنا بكلِّ مالِهِ لمسلم أو ذمي) ، فإنَّ الوصية بكلِّ المال إنما لا تصح لحق الورثة ، وأما المستأمن فورثته في دار الحرب، وهم في حكم الأموات، فلا مانع من الصحة. معلوم، كما لو أوصى بغير البيعة والكنيسة، وإذا صار ملكاً لهم يصنعون به ما شاؤوا. كذا في «الجلبي» [1] قوله : لا عندهما ... الخ ؛ قال في «الكفاية» : قال مشايخنا : هذا الاختلافُ فيما إذا أوصى ببناء بيعة أو كنيسة في القرى، أما في المصر فلا تجوز بالاتفاق ؛ لأنهم لا يمكنون عن إحدث البيعة في الأمصار. كذا في «الجامع الصغير» للربّاني . [2] قوله : لا وارث له هنا ؛ أي في دارنا ومفهومه لو كان وارثه هنا لا تجوز بأكثر من الثلث، وعبّر الزيلعي وغيره عن هذا المفهوم بلفظ : قيل، فأفاد ضعفه لكن جزم بما ذكره المتون ؛ لأن المتون مقدمة على الشروح. و به جزم الإتقاني له مستندا إلى ما في «شرح السِّرَخْسِي» ؛ لأنَّ حق وارثه في دارنا معتبر بسبب الأمان، ولو كان له وارث ثمة آخر شارك الحاضر، ولم يكن للموصي له إلا الثلث. كذا في «رد المحتار).
The will of a musta'min who has no heir here (in our land) giving all his wealth to a Muslim or a dhimmi. According to Abu Hanifa, it is valid for him; according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is not, for a will to commit a sin is not valid. His (Abu Hanifa's) proof: it is an act of piety in their belief, and they are left to follow their religion. (The will of a musta'min who has no heir here with all his wealth to a Muslim or a dhimmi) — indeed, the will of all wealth is not valid only due to the right of heirs. As for the musta'min, his heirs are in dar al-harb, and they are considered as dead, so there is no impediment to validity. This is known, just as if he bequeathed other than a place of allegiance (bay'a) or a church. And when it becomes their property, they may do with it as they wish. This is in al-Jalabi. [1] His statement: 'not according to the two' etc. He said in al-Kifaya: Our scholars said: This difference is when he bequeaths the building of a place of allegiance or a church in villages. As for a metropolis (misr), it is not permissible by consensus, because they are not permitted to establish places of allegiance in the metropolises. This is in al-Jami' al-Saghir by al-Rabbani. [2] His statement: 'no heir here' means in our land. Its implication: if his heir were here, it would not be permissible for more than a third. al-Zayla'i and others expressed this implication with the word 'qila', indicating its weakness, but he affirmed what the texts (mutun) mentioned, because the texts take precedence over commentaries. al-Itqani affirmed it, relying on what is in Sharh al-Sarakhsi, because the right of his heir in our land is considered due to the aman (safe conduct). And if he had an heir there (in dar al-harb) who shared with the present heir, the legatee would not have more than a third. This is in Radd al-Muhtar.
Source pages p. 629

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a musta'min's will. The musta'min (a person given safe conduct into Muslim lands) has no heirs in Muslim territory. He bequeaths all his wealth to a Muslim or a dhimmi. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, this is valid. The two leading disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagree, arguing that bequeathing for a sinful purpose is invalid, and building churches or synagogues is sinful in Islam. Abu Hanifa responds that such acts are considered pious in the musta'min's own belief, and non-Muslims are allowed to follow their religion. The commentary then notes that the disagreement is limited to rural areas (qura); in a metropolis (misr) it is unanimously prohibited because non-Muslims are not allowed to establish religious buildings there. Additionally, the condition 'no heir here' implies that if the musta'min had an heir in Muslim territory, the will would be restricted to one-third (due to the heir's right). This implication is considered weak by some, but the matn (core text) is authoritative over commentaries.

Key Terms
مستأمن (musta'min): A non-Muslim who enters Muslim territory with a safe conduct (aman).
وصية بالمعصية (wasiyya bil-ma'siya): A bequest for an act considered sinful in Islam, such as building a church.
قربة (qurba): An act of piety or closeness to God according to the testator's belief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which area is the musta'min's will for building a church unanimously invalid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Villages (qura)
B) Metropolises (amsar)
C) Both areas
D) Neither area

Key Takeaway
The validity of a musta'min's will depends on whether the bequest is considered qurba in his belief and on the location—metropolises have a consensus of invalidity.
