Acceptance and Rejection of Executorship
Rulings on acceptance and rejection of executorship after appointment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for valid rejection of executorship and explain the legal effect of the executor's silence until the testator's death.

Core Idea
Rejection of executorship only counts in the testator's presence; silence leaves full choice after death.

Being appointed as an executor (wasi) is a serious responsibility. The Hanafi school carefully regulates how acceptance and rejection of this role are legally effective. This lesson unpacks the key rules: rejection must occur in the testator's presence to protect against deception, and silence does not bind the executor—he may decide after the testator's death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Locate the matn: 'If he rejects in his presence, it is rejection; otherwise not.' Understand that the testator's presence is a condition for a valid rejection.
The ruling distinguishes between rejection in presence (valid) and absence (invalid).
2
Unpack the rationale
Reflect on why rejection in absence is invalid. The text says it would cause deception (ghurur) because the testator relied on the executor's acceptance.
The testator may not appoint another if he believes the executor has accepted.
3
Analyze the case of silence
Examine the second ruling: if the executor remains silent until the testator dies, he retains the choice to accept or reject afterward. The testator's negligence in not seeking a clear answer does not remove this option.
Silence during the testator's lifetime is not acceptance.
4
Consider the objections
Follow the commentary's objection: silence could harm the testator who relies on the executor. Then see the reply: the testator should have asked explicitly; his inaction preserves the executor's choice.
This demonstrates Hanafi istihsan (juristic preference) to prevent injustice.
5
Summarize the practical takeaway
An executor who wishes to reject must do so while the testator is alive and in his presence. If he remains silent, he is free to accept or reject after death.
There is no automatic acceptance by silence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الوصي يقال : أوصى إلى فلان : أي فوَّضَ إِليه التَّصرُّفَ في ماله بعد موته ، والاسم منه الوصاية بالكسر والفتح، والمفوض إليه الوصي. (ومن أوصى إلى زيد وقبل عنده ، فإن ردّ عنده ردّ" وإلا لا ) ، وإنما لا صحُ الرَّدُّ بغيبته ؛ لأنه اعتمد عليه حيث قبله بحضوره ، فإن صح الرَّدُّ بغيبته يلزمُ الغرور، فإن سكت فمات موصيه ، فله رده وضده) : أي القبول. [1] قوله : باب الوصي ؛ وهو الموصى إليه ، لما فرغ من بيان الموصى له شرع في بيان أحكام الموصى إليه، وهو الوصي، لما أنّ كتاب الوصايا يشمله، لكن قدَّمَ أحكام الموصى له ؛ لكثرتها وكثرة وقوعها ، فكانت الحاجة إلى معرفتها أمس عناية. واعلم أنه لا ينبغي للوصي أن يقبلها ؛ لأنها على خطر، وعن أبي يوسف له : الدخول فيها أوّل مرّة ،غلط، والثانية خيانة ، والثالثة سرقة ، وعن الحسن : لا يقدر الوصي أن يعدل ولو كان عمر بن الخطاب له ، وقال أبو مطيع : ما رأيت في مدة قضائي عشرين سنة من يعدل في مال ابن أخيه. «قُهستاني». كذا في «الرد المحتار). [2] قوله : فإن ردّ عنده ردّ ؛ أي إن ردَّ زيد عند الموصي فهو ردّ ؛ لأنه ليس للموصي ولاية إلزامِهِ التصرف، ولا غرور فيه ؛ لأنه يمكنه أن ينيب غيره. [3] قوله : فإن سكت... الخ ؛ أي أن يستقبل ولم يرد حتى مات الموصي ، فله الخيار إن شاء قبل وإن شاء ردّ ؛ لأن الموصي ليس له ولاية الإلزام كما مر ، فبقي مخيراً. فإن قيل : يجب أن لا يكون مخيراً ؛ لأنه لما بلغه الإيصاء ولم يرده اعتمد على الموصي، ولم يوص إلى غيره، وفي ذلك ضرر به، والضرر مدفوع. وأجيب عليه : بأن الموصي مفترِ حيث لم يسأله من الرد والقبول فلا يبطل الخيار. كذا في «العناية».
Chapter of the Executor. It is said: 'awsa ila flan' meaning he delegated to him the disposition of his property after his death. The noun from it is al-wisaya (with kasr or fath of the waw), and the one delegated to is al-wasi. (And whoever appoints Zayd as executor and he accepts in his presence, then if he rejects in his presence, it is rejection; otherwise not.) It is not valid to reject in his absence because he relied on him having accepted in his presence. If rejection in his absence were valid, it would lead to deception. (If he remains silent and then his testator dies, he has the choice of acceptance or rejection, i.e., acceptance.) [1] His statement: 'Chapter of the Executor'; he is the one appointed. When he finished explaining the legatee, he began explaining the rulings of the appointee, i.e., the executor, because the Book of Wills includes it. But he presented the rulings of the legatee first due to their abundance and frequency, so the need to know them was more pressing. Know that it is not proper for the executor to accept it because it is risky. From Abu Yusuf: entering into it the first time is a mistake, the second is betrayal, and the third is theft. From al-Hasan: the executor cannot be just even if he were Umar ibn al-Khattab. Abu Muti' said: In my twenty years of judgeship, I have not seen anyone who was just in handling the wealth of his brother's son. Qahastani. This is in Radd al-Muhtar. [2] His statement: 'if he rejects in his presence, it is rejection' meaning if Zayd rejects in the presence of the testator, it is rejection, because the testator does not have the authority to compel him to act, and there is no deception because he can appoint someone else. [3] His statement: 'if he remains silent...' meaning he postponed and did not reject until the testator died, then he has the option: if he wishes he may accept and if he wishes he may reject, because the testator does not have the authority to compel him, as previously, so he remains free to choose. If it is said: he should not have the choice because when the appointment reached him and he did not reject it, the testator relied on him and did not appoint someone else, and that causes harm, and harm is to be prevented. The answer: the testator is negligent for not asking him to accept or reject, so the choice is not nullified. This is in al-`Inaya.
Source pages p. 630

Commentary
This passage begins the chapter on the executor (wasi). It defines the term: 'awsa ila fulan' means delegating to someone the authority to dispose of one's property after death. The verbal noun is wisaya, and the person appointed is wasi. The key ruling: If a testator appoints Zayd as executor and Zayd accepts in the testator's presence, then a subsequent rejection is only valid if done in the testator's presence. Rejection in absence is not valid because the testator relied on the acceptance, and allowing such rejection would deceive the testator. If Zayd is silent (neither accepts nor rejects) until the testator dies, then Zayd has the choice to accept or reject after death. The commentary expands on the wisdom of the chapter arrangement and includes warnings from Abu Yusuf, al-Hasan, and Abu Muti' about the difficulty of being a just executor. It also addresses a potential objection: if the executor remains silent, the testator may rely on him and not appoint another, causing harm. The answer is that the testator should have asked for a clear response; his negligence does not remove the executor's choice.

Key Terms
وصي (wasi): The person appointed by a testator to manage his property after death; the executor.
رد (radd): Rejection (of the executorship); legally effective only if done in the testator's presence.
سكت (sakata): Remained silent (neither accepted nor rejected) until the testator's death; results in a choice after death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is rejection of executorship invalid if done in the testator's absence?
A) Because the executor must always accept the role.
B) Because the testator relied on the executor's acceptance in his presence, and allowing rejection in absence would cause deception.
C) Because the executor cannot change his mind once he has accepted.
D) Because the testator is dead and cannot hear the rejection.

Key Takeaway
An executor's rejection must be in the testator's presence to be valid; silence leaves the executor free to choose after the testator's death.

Rulings on the Executor: Acceptance, Rejection, and Judicial Oversight
Explaining rulings for executor: definition, acceptance modes, and invalid appointees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for valid acceptance and rejection of executorship, explain the legal effect of silence until the testator's death, and identify when a judge may replace an appointed executor.

Core Idea
An executor's acceptance must be explicit in the testator's presence; silence until death leaves the choice open.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the rulings for the executor (al-wasi) in Hanafi law. It covers how acceptance and rejection are valid only in certain contexts, the effect of silence, and the judge's authority to replace unfit executors. The commentary resolves a potential contradiction regarding the appointment of slaves, disbelievers, and sinners.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what executorship (wasaya) is and whether accepting it is obligatory, recommended, or permissible. Note the Hanafi position that acceptance is permissible (mubah) but discouraged due to risk.
The text quotes Abu Yusuf and others cautioning against accepting executorship.
2
Analyze the modes of acceptance and rejection
If the testator appoints someone in their presence and that person accepts, rejection must also be in the testator's presence to be valid. Rejection in absence is invalid because it would deceive the testator. If the person remains silent until the testator dies, they retain the option to accept or reject.
The commentary explains that the testator has no authority to compel acceptance; the choice remains because the testator should have asked for a clear answer.
3
Examine the sale of estate property without knowledge
If the executor sells estate property without knowing about the will, the sale is binding. Compare this to an agent who sells without knowledge of the agency – that sale is not binding. The difference is that executorship is a transfer of authority after death, like inheritance, not dependent on knowledge.
This illustrates the unique nature of executorship as opposed to agency.
4
Understand the dispute on unfit executors
If a slave (not the testator's own), a disbeliever, or a sinful person is appointed, the judge may substitute them. The Hanafi majority holds that the appointment is valid but the judge can remove them. Imam Muhammad reportedly considers such a will invalid. The commentary reconciles this by noting that al-Quduri's phrase 'removes them' implies that removal occurs only after entry, indicating validity.
The practical consequence: actions of such an executor before removal may be valid depending on the ruling adopted.

Chapter of the Executor. He who appoints Zayd as executor, and (Zayd) accepts in his (the testator's) presence, then if he rejects in his presence it is valid, otherwise not. If he remains silent and then the testator dies, he has the right to reject or accept. The chapter of the executor: [1] It is said: 'appointed so-and-so as executor' means he delegated to him the disposition of his property after his death. The noun form is 'al-wasaya' with kasra or fatha, and the delegate is 'al-wasi'. (Whoever appoints Zayd as executor and he accepts in his presence, then if he rejects in his presence it is valid, otherwise not). Rejection in his absence is not valid because (the testator) relied on him when he accepted in his presence; if rejection in his absence were valid it would cause deception. If he remains silent (3) and then the testator dies, he has the right to reject or accept, i.e., acceptance. R. [1] His statement: 'Chapter of the executor' – he is the one to whom the will is made. After finishing the explanation of the legatee, he began explaining the rulings of the one to whom the will is made, i.e., the executor, because the book of wills includes it. However, he presented the rulings of the legatee first due to their abundance and frequency. Know that the executor should not accept it because it is risky. From Abu Yusuf: entering it the first time is a mistake, the second is treachery, the third is theft. From al-Hasan: the executor cannot be just even if he were Umar ibn al-Khattab. Abu Muti' said: I have not seen in my twenty years of judging anyone who is just regarding the wealth of his brother's son. 'Quhistani'. Likewise in 'Radd al-Muhtar'. [2] His statement: 'If he rejects in his presence it is valid' – meaning if Zayd rejects before the testator then it is valid rejection because the testator has no authority to force him to act, and there is no deception in it because he can appoint someone else. [3] His statement: 'If he remains silent...' – meaning he remained silent and did not reject until the testator died; he has the option: if he wishes he may accept, if he wishes he may reject, because the testator has no authority to compel him as mentioned, so he remains with choice. If it is said: he should not have a choice because when he was informed of the will and did not reject, the testator relied on him and did not appoint another, causing harm to him, and harm is repelled. The answer: the testator is negligent for not asking him to reject or accept, so the choice is not invalidated. Thus in 'al-Inayah' (2). (1) Radd al-Muhtar 6:700-701. (2) al-Inayah 10:498. [PAGE_631] Book of Wills/Definitions and Rulings 631. And (the executor's) sale of some of the estate is binding even if he was unaware of it. If he rejects after his (the testator's) death then later accepts, it is valid unless the judge has nullified his rejection. And (if the testator appoints) a slave, a disbeliever, or a sinful person, the judge substitutes them with someone else. [1] His statement: 'the sale is binding because it comes from the executor contrary to the agent' etc. – it is not binding because executorship differs; it is specific to the time when the authority of the deceased is severed, so authority transfers to him. And since it is different, it does not depend on knowledge like inheritance. As for agency, it is a deputyship established while the principal's authority exists, so it is not valid without his knowledge, like establishing ownership by sale and purchase. Thus in 'al-Hidayah'. [2] His statement: 'since by mere rejection...' – explanation: because by mere rejection the will is not nullified, for its nullification would harm the deceased, and the harm to the executor in maintaining the will is compensated by reward. Repelling the former is more important. However, if the judge removes him from executorship it is valid because it is a matter of ijtihad, and the judge has authority to repel harm from people. Sometimes the executor is unable to (fulfill) and is harmed by remaining executorship, so the judge removes the harm from him, and (the judge) appoints a guardian of the deceased's property acting in it, so harm is repelled from both sides. Hence his removal is binding. If he (the executor) says after the judge's removal, it is not heeded because he accepted after the executorship was nullified by the judge. Thus in 'al-Hidayah' (3). [3] His statement: 'a slave' – meaning a slave other than the testator's own, supported by his later statement: 'or his own slave'. [4] His statement: 'the judge substitutes them with someone else' – i.e., the judge removes them from executorship and appoints (another). (1) This will is invalid according to what Muhammad (al-Shaybani) stated; the expression of al-Quduri is: 'the judge removes them from the will', indicating that the will is valid because removal occurs only after entry. See: Fath Bab al-Inayah 3:440. (2) al-Hidayah 4:258. (3) al-Hidayah 4:258.
Source pages p. 630

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary (sharh) on al-Wiqayah, explaining the chapter on the executor (al-wasi). The author begins by defining the executor as the one to whom disposition of property after death is delegated. The key ruling: if the testator declares someone as executor and that person accepts in his presence, then any rejection must also be in his presence to be valid; rejection in absence is invalid because it would be deceptive. If the person remains silent until the testator's death, he retains the option to accept or reject afterward. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: since the testator relied on the executor's silence, why allow rejection later? The answer is that the testator should have explicitly asked for acceptance or rejection; his failure to do so means the choice remains. The passage also discusses the validity of the executor's sale of estate property without knowledge of the will: it is binding because executorship is like a transfer of authority upon death, not dependent on knowledge. Finally, if a slave (not the testator's own), a disbeliever, or a sinful person is appointed, the judge has the authority to remove them and appoint another. This is because the judge's power to prevent harm overrides the initial appointment. The commentary draws from authorities like al-Hidayah, al-Inayah, and Radd al-Muhtar, and mentions a difference between Muhammad al-Shaybani and al-Quduri regarding the validity of such appointments.

Key Terms
الوصي (al-wasiyy): The person appointed (by will) to manage the deceased's estate after death.
الوصاية (al-wasaya): The office or function of being an executor.
الإيصاء (al-isa'): The act of making a will, specifically appointing an executor.
الرد (ar-radd): Rejection of the executorship by the appointed person.
القبول (al-qabul): Acceptance of the executorship by the appointed person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person is appointed executor in the presence of the testator and remains silent until the testator dies, what is his legal situation?
A) He is obligated to accept the executorship because his silence implies acceptance.
B) He has the option to either accept or reject the executorship.
C) He must reject immediately; otherwise he is liable.
D) The executorship becomes void automatically.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an executor's silence after appointment does not bind him; he may accept or reject later, and the judge may replace an unfit executor to prevent harm.

Wasiyyah — Bequest

Rulings on Executor's Sale, Rejection, and Appointment of Non-Ideal Executors
Rulings on executor's actions and appointment of imperfect executors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an executor's sale without knowledge of the bequest, distinguish it from agency, and explain the validity of appointing a slave, disbeliever, or sinner as executor with the qadi's role.

Core Idea
An executor's authority is like inheritance—valid without knowledge; a deficient executor is valid initially but removable by the qadi.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses several practical questions about executors: What if the executor sells estate property without knowing he was appointed? What if he rejects the role after the testator's death? And who can be appointed as executor—a slave, a disbeliever, or a sinner? The Hanafi school treats these appointments as valid in principle, but the qadi has the authority to replace them if needed. Let us examine each ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on sale without knowledge
The text states the executor's sale is valid even if he was unaware of his appointment, unlike an agent. Note the key distinction: executorship is a transfer of authority independent of the executor's knowledge, similar to inheritance. Agency requires knowledge because it is a deputation from a living principal.
This is a unique feature of Hanafi law; other schools may treat executorship more like agency.
2
Analyze the rule on rejection and acceptance
Mere rejection after death does not nullify the executorship; the judge's removal is required. Why? To prevent harm to the deceased. The harm to the executor in being forced to serve is outweighed by the reward of executing the will. If the judge removes him, the removal is effective.
The judge has authority to remove harm from people.
3
Examine the appointment of a slave, disbeliever, or sinner
The matn says the judge replaces them. The commentary clarifies that the appointment is initially valid because the basis of oversight (asl al-nazar) exists in all: the slave is a responsible person, the sinner has authority in Hanafi law, the disbeliever has general authority. But specific deficiencies (slave depends on master, disbeliever has enmity, sinner is untrustworthy) cause the qadi to complete oversight by removal.
Some Hanafi authorities (e.g., Imam Muhammad) consider the will void ab initio, but the relied-upon view is valid subject to qadi removal.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
There are two positions: (1) The will is void from the start (Imam Muhammad, al-Sarakhsi). (2) The will is valid but the qadi will remove the executor (al-Quduri and the commentary). The practical difference: if the executor dies before removal, his actions are valid under the second view; under the first, they are void.
The text reconciles by stating that removal implies prior validity, favoring the second view.

Source pages p. 631

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Wasiyyah (Wills) in 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses three main rulings: (1) The effect of an executor selling estate property without knowledge of the will, (2) The validity of a rejection of executorship after the testator's death, and (3) The appointment of various persons as executor—specifically a slave (not owned by the testator), a disbeliever, a sinner, and one's own slave. The text is a commentary (sharh) on the matn, expanding and explaining the rulings and their rationales.

First, regarding sale without knowledge: the commentary explains that if an executor sells something from the estate without knowing of his appointment, the sale is still valid. This is unlike an agent (wakil) who sells without knowledge of his agency—such a sale is not valid. The reason is that executorship (wisaya) is a transfer of authority from the deceased to the executor, independent of the executor's knowledge, similar to inheritance. Agency, on the other hand, is a deputation from a living principal and requires the agent's knowledge for its validity.

Second, if a person designated as executor rejects the role after the testator's death and later accepts, his acceptance is valid. However, if the judge (qadi) has already removed him (i.e., ruled that he is not the executor), then his later acceptance is ineffective. The mere rejection does not nullify the executorship because nullification would harm the deceased (by leaving the estate unmanaged). The harm to the executor in being forced to serve is outweighed by the reward of executing the will. However, if the judge removes him due to harm or inability, that removal is effective because the judge has the authority to prevent harm.

Third, the appointment of a slave (not owned by the testator), a disbeliever, or a sinner as executor: the matn states that the judge replaces them with someone else. The commentary notes a difference of opinion on whether the original appointment is valid or invalid from the start. According to one view (ascribed to Imam Muhammad), the will to such persons is void ab initio. Another view (favored by al-Quduri and others) holds that it is valid but the judge will remove them. The text reconciles these by stating that the basis of oversight (ahl al-nazar) exists in all of them—the slave is capable, the sinner has authority according to Hanafi principles, and the disbeliever has general authority—but due to specific deficiencies (slave's dependence on master, disbeliever's enmity, sinner's untrustworthiness), the qadi completes the oversight by replacing them.

Fourth, the appointment of one's own slave as executor: if the testator's heirs are minors, Abu Hanifa rules it valid; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) rule it invalid based on qiyas (analogy). The commentary explains Abu Hanifa's proof: the slave has compassion for his master's children that no one else has, and the minor heirs, though owners, have no authority to prevent the slave from acting. The Sahibayn argue that the slave cannot have authority over his master's estate because the slave himself is property, and giving him authority over the heirs (who are owners) inverts the natural order. The text ultimately supports the view that the will is valid but subject to judicial removal if needed. The relied-upon position is that after the qadi removes a deficient executor, the will is considered void from that point.

Key Terms
الوصاية (al-wisāyah): The office of executor; a charge given to a person to manage the testator's estate after death.
الوصي (al-waṣī): The executor appointed to manage the will and estate.
الرد (al-radd): Rejection of the executorship by the designated person after the testator's death.
الإخراج (al-ikhrāj): Removal of the executor by the qadi due to incapacity or harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a testator appoints a disbeliever as executor, what is the ruling?
A) The appointment is void from the start because a disbeliever cannot have authority over a Muslim's estate.
B) The appointment is valid but the qadi will replace the disbeliever with a Muslim executor.
C) The appointment is valid only if the disbeliever is a relative.
D) The appointment is valid only if all heirs are adults.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the appointment of a slave, disbeliever, or sinner as executor is valid initially, but the qadi has the authority to replace them if their deficiencies impair estate management.

Wasiyyah — Bequest
intermediate
8 min
Appointing One's Own Slave as Executor: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on appointing one's own slave as executor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the validity of appointing one's own slave as executor for minor heirs, and state the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa allows appointing one's own slave for minor heirs due to compassion; the Sahibayn forbid it as an inversion of the prescribed order.

A significant intra-Hanafi dispute arises when a testator appoints his own slave as executor over his minor children. Abu Hanifa holds this appointment valid, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani deem it invalid based on qiyas. The passage presents both arguments and resolves the issue by acknowledging that the appointment is valid but subject to qadi removal. Let us examine the reasoning of each side.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi positions
Abu Hanifa: the appointment is valid if the heirs are minors. The Sahibayn: it is invalid even if the heirs are minors. This ikhtilaf is the focus of this lesson.
The text says 'this is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two said: it is not valid even if the heirs are minors.'
2
Follow the Sahibayn's proof (qiyas)
The Sahibayn argue by analogy: authority cannot be given to a slave over his master's estate because slavery negates full legal capacity. Establishing authority of a slave over his master's property inverts the prescribed order (qalb al-mashru'). Moreover, the slave cannot sell his own person, so his authority over the estate is incomplete.
This is based on the principle that the slave is property, not an owner.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa argues that the slave is a responsible person (mukhatab) and has capacity to transact. The slave has unique compassion for the testator's children. The minor heirs, though owners, have no authority to prevent the slave from acting. The testator's bequest itself indicates consent. These factors justify validity.
The text says 'his proof: that his slave has compassion that no one else has.'
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa's view, the slave's actions as executor are valid from the start. Under the Sahibayn's view, any actions are invalid. The text reconciles by stating that the will is valid until the qadi removes the slave. Thus, the practical effect is that the slave acts initially, but if the qadi removes him, his authority ends.
The text says 'and this indicates the validity of the will; because removal comes after it.'
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The commentary leans towards Abu Hanifa's view as the basis for the rule that removal presupposes validity. The mufta bihi is that the appointment of one's own slave for minor heirs is valid, but the qadi may remove the slave if necessary.
This is consistent with the general principle that deficient executors are dealt with by qadi removal.

Indeed, if the executor sells something from the estate without knowledge of the bequest, the sale is effective, unlike an agent who sells something without knowledge of the agency. (Then if he rejects after his death then accepts, it is valid except if the judge's rejection is executed). Because mere rejection does not nullify the executorship; since its nullification would harm the deceased, unless that is confirmed by the judge's ruling. (And to a slave, or a disbeliever, or a sinner, the judge replaces him with another). It is said: the executorship [is invalid]. [Commentary: The sale is effective because it originates from the executor, unlike the agent... because executorship is unlike agency; it is specific to the situation where the deceased's authority is cut off, so authority transfers to him. And since it is like that, it does not depend on knowledge like inheritance. As for agency, it is a deputation; established while the principal's authority is ongoing, so it is not valid without his knowledge, like establishing ownership through sale and purchase. Thus in al-Hidayah. [Commentary: Merely rejection... explanation: mere rejection does not nullify the bequest; because nullification would harm the deceased, and the harm to the executor in keeping the bequest is compensated by reward, and preventing the first is higher and more appropriate, except that if the judge removes him from executorship, it is valid; because the judge has authority to remove harm from people. And perhaps the executor is unable to do that, so he is harmed by the continuation of executorship, so the judge removes the harm from him, and appoints a guardian of the deceased's property acting in it, so harm is removed from both sides; thus his removal is effective. So if he says after the judge's removal, it is not heeded; because he accepted after the nullification of executorship by the judge's annulment. Thus in al-Hidayah.] [Commentary: 'And to a slave' i.e., a slave not owned by the testator, supported by his saying later: 'or to his own slave'. [Commentary: 'the judge replaces him with another' i.e., the judge removes them from executorship and appoints... This bequest is invalid according to what Muhammad mentioned, and the expression of al-Quduri: 'the judge removes them from the will', indicating the will is valid because removal only occurs after entry. See Fath Bab al-Inaya. Al-Hidayah. Hassan al-Diraya li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqaya. And it is said: regarding the slave it is invalid, regarding others it is valid. And it is said: regarding the disbeliever it is invalid due to lack of authority over the Muslim, regarding others it is valid. (And to his own slave it is valid if his heirs are minors, otherwise not). This is according to Abu Hanifa. And the two said: It is not valid even if the heirs are minors, and that is the analogy; because it inverts the prescribed. His proof: that his slave has compassion that no one else has, and the minors, even though they are owners, have no one else. The face of analogy: that authority is non-existent in his own slave, because slavery negates it; and because it establishes authority of the slave over the master, which inverts the prescribed; and because the authority originates from the father, and in considering this, it is incomplete because he cannot sell his own person, and this contradicts the subject. Thus in al-Hidayah. 2 His saying: his proof... because the slave is a responsible person independent in transactions, so he is fit for executorship. Indeed, the minors even if they are owners, they have no authority to prevent, so there is no contradiction. And the testator's bequest to him indicates [the testator's consent].
Source pages p. 631

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Wasiyyah (Wills) in 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses three main rulings: (1) The effect of an executor selling estate property without knowledge of the will, (2) The validity of a rejection of executorship after the testator's death, and (3) The appointment of various persons as executor—specifically a slave (not owned by the testator), a disbeliever, a sinner, and one's own slave. The text is a commentary (sharh) on the matn, expanding and explaining the rulings and their rationales.

First, regarding sale without knowledge: the commentary explains that if an executor sells something from the estate without knowing of his appointment, the sale is still valid. This is unlike an agent (wakil) who sells without knowledge of his agency—such a sale is not valid. The reason is that executorship (wisaya) is a transfer of authority from the deceased to the executor, independent of the executor's knowledge, similar to inheritance. Agency, on the other hand, is a deputation from a living principal and requires the agent's knowledge for its validity.

Second, if a person designated as executor rejects the role after the testator's death and later accepts, his acceptance is valid. However, if the judge (qadi) has already removed him (i.e., ruled that he is not the executor), then his later acceptance is ineffective. The mere rejection does not nullify the executorship because nullification would harm the deceased (by leaving the estate unmanaged). The harm to the executor in being forced to serve is outweighed by the reward of executing the will. However, if the judge removes him due to harm or inability, that removal is effective because the judge has the authority to prevent harm.

Third, the appointment of a slave (not owned by the testator), a disbeliever, or a sinner as executor: the matn states that the judge replaces them with someone else. The commentary notes a difference of opinion on whether the original appointment is valid or invalid from the start. According to one view (ascribed to Imam Muhammad), the will to such persons is void ab initio. Another view (favored by al-Quduri and others) holds that it is valid but the judge will remove them. The text reconciles these by stating that the basis of oversight (ahl al-nazar) exists in all of them—the slave is capable, the sinner has authority according to Hanafi principles, and the disbeliever has general authority—but due to specific deficiencies (slave's dependence on master, disbeliever's enmity, sinner's untrustworthiness), the qadi completes the oversight by replacing them.

Fourth, the appointment of one's own slave as executor: if the testator's heirs are minors, Abu Hanifa rules it valid; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) rule it invalid based on qiyas (analogy). The commentary explains Abu Hanifa's proof: the slave has compassion for his master's children that no one else has, and the minor heirs, though owners, have no authority to prevent the slave from acting. The Sahibayn argue that the slave cannot have authority over his master's estate because the slave himself is property, and giving him authority over the heirs (who are owners) inverts the natural order. The text ultimately supports the view that the will is valid but subject to judicial removal if needed. The relied-upon position is that after the qadi removes a deficient executor, the will is considered void from that point.

Key Terms
الوصاية (al-wisāyah): The office of executor; a charge given to a person to manage the testator's estate after death.
الوصي (al-waṣī): The executor appointed to manage the will and estate.
الرد (al-radd): Rejection of the executorship by the designated person after the testator's death.
الإخراج (al-ikhrāj): Removal of the executor by the qadi due to incapacity or harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, under what condition is appointing one's own slave as executor valid?
A) When the slave is literate
B) When the heirs are all minors
C) When the heirs are all adults
D) When the testator has no other relatives

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accommodates the appointment of one's own slave as executor for minor heirs, prioritizing compassion and practical need over formal legal capacity, with qadi oversight as a safeguard.

Slave Executorship with Minor Heirs
Ruling on the validity of a slave's executorship with minor heirs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on slave executorship and apply the rule regarding minor heirs.

Core Idea
A slave's executorship is valid if heirs are minors, based on istihsan, not invalid outright.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute. While the Sahibayn hold that a slave cannot serve as executor due to lack of ownership capacity, Abu Hanifa validates the appointment when the heirs are minors, relying on the slave's unique affection and the need for their welfare. The discussion also touches on the role of the judge in removing the executor if needed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling and its condition
The passage states: 'And to his slave it is valid if his heirs are minors, otherwise not.' Determine the act (appointing a slave as executor) and the condition (heirs are minors) that triggers validity.
Note the phrase 'otherwise not' – the ruling flips when heirs are adults.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
The text contrasts Abu Hanifa's view with that of the Sahibayn. Identify each position: Abu Hanifa says valid with minor heirs; the two disciples say invalid even then.
The Sahibayn argue by analogy (qiyas) because it 'reverses the prescribed law'.
3
Analyze the proofs (dalil)
Abu Hanifa’s proof (istihsan) is the slave's special affection for the minors. The Sahibayn’s proof (qiyas) is that executorship requires ownership capacity, which a slave lacks. Evaluate the reasoning.
Istihsan prefers a beneficial outcome over strict analogy.
4
Understand the role of the judge
The passage clarifies that the will becomes invalid only by the judge's removal. This means the initial appointment is valid, but the judge can later remove the slave if supervision is compromised.
This is the practical consequence of Abu Hanifa's position: validity subject to judicial oversight.
5
Synthesize the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the will is initially valid and only voided by the judge; under the Sahibayn, it is void from the start. Identify the practical difference in estate management.
The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view, as preferred in al-Hidayah.

(وإلى عبده صح إن كان ورثته صغاراً وإلا لا)، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ، وقالا : لا تصح وإن كانت الورثة صغاراً ، وهو القياس؛ لأنه قَلَبَ المشروع. له : أن لعبده من الشفقة ما لا يكون لغيره، والصغار وإن كانوا ملاكا ليس غيرهم، وهذا يشير إلى صحة الوصيّة ؛ لأنَّ الإخراج يكون بعدها لما ذكر محمد له في «المبسوط»: إنّ الوصيّة باطلة ، فاختلف الفقهاء في معنى قوله. فقال الفقيه أبو الليث : معناه في جميع هذه الصور أن الوصية ستبطل بإخراجه القاضي عنها ، وقيل في العبد معناه باطل حقيقة ؛ لعدم ولايته، وفي غيره : معناه ستبطل، وذهب إليه شمس الأئمة السرخسي ، وقيل : في الكافر باطل أيضاً ؛ لعدم ولايته على المسلم. ووجه الصحة ثم الإخراج أنَّ أصل النظر ثابت ؛ لقـدرة الـعـبـد حقيقة وولاية الفاسق على أصلنا، وولاية الكافر في الجملة، إلا أنه لم يتم النظر ؛ لتوقف ولاية العبدِ على إجازة المولى، وتمكنه من الحجر بعدها، والمعاداة الدينية الباعثة للكافر على ترك النظر في حق المسلم، واتهام الفاسق بالخيانة، فيخرجه القاضي من الوصاية، ويقيم غيره مقامه ؛ إتماماً للنظر. كذا في «الهداية».
And to his slave it is valid if his heirs are minors, otherwise not. It becomes invalid only by the judge's removal. It is said: in the case of a slave, it is invalid; in others, valid. It is said: in the case of a disbeliever, invalid due to lack of guardianship over a Muslim; in others valid. (And to his slave it is valid if his heirs are minors, otherwise not) – this is according to Abu Hanifa. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say: it is not valid even if the heirs are minors, and that is the analogy because it reverses the prescribed law. For him (Abu Hanifa) is that his slave has affection that others do not have, and the minors, even if they are owners, are not others. This indicates the validity of the will, because the removal occurs after it. When Muhammad mentioned to him in al-Mabsut: the will is invalid, the jurists disagreed on the meaning of his statement. Abu al-Layth said: its meaning in all these forms is that the will will become invalid by the judge's removal. It was said regarding a slave it is invalid in reality due to lack of guardianship, and regarding others it means it will become invalid. Shams al-A'immah al-Sarakhsi held this view. It was said regarding a disbeliever it is also invalid due to lack of guardianship over a Muslim. Source pages p. 632

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of appointing a slave as an executor (wasi) when the heirs are minors. The main ruling states that if the heirs are minors, the appointment is valid; if they are adults, it is not. The commentary clarifies that the will becomes invalid only when the judge removes the slave executor. There are variant opinions: some say it is inherently invalid if the executor is a slave, valid otherwise; some say invalid if the executor is a disbeliever. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the appointment of a slave as executor is valid when the heirs are minors because of the slave's affection and trustworthiness. However, the two companions (Sahibayn) hold it invalid even in that case, based on analogy (qiyas) since it reverses the natural order of ownership. Abu Hanifa relies on istihsan, considering the slave's special relationship with the minors. The judge is then expected to remove the slave if he is found incapable, but the initial appointment is sound. This reflects the Hanafi principle of preferring the deceased's choice unless there is clear harm.

Key Terms
وصي (wasi): Executor or guardian appointed to manage the deceased's estate and fulfill the will.
ولاية (wilayah): Legal authority or guardianship to act on behalf of another.
نظر (nazar): Supervision and consideration in managing the estate for the benefit of the heirs.
إستحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides strict analogy for a more equitable outcome.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The slave's affection and trustworthiness towards the minors
C) The judge's permission prior to appointment
D) The master's guarantee of the slave's performance

Key Takeaway
A slave's appointment as executor is valid if heirs are minors, based on istihsan; the judge may later remove him if supervision is compromised.

Judge Appointing Assistant for Incapable Executor
Ruling on judge's duty regarding executor capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on appointing a co-executor for an incapable executor and apply the condition for retaining a capable executor.

Core Idea
The judge must assist an incapable executor, not replace him; and never remove a capable one.

This passage from al-Hidayah clarifies the judge's duty when the appointed executor cannot perform. It balances the deceased's right to choose with the need to safeguard the estate. The judge's response depends on the executor's capacity and trustworthiness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text distinguishes between an executor who is incapable of carrying out the duties and one who is capable and trustworthy. Recognize these two cases.
2
State the judge's action for the incapable executor
For the incapable executor, the judge must join (damm) another person as co-executor, not remove the original one. The phrase 'ḍumma ilayhi ghayruh' means another is added.
The original executor is not removed; the judge adds a helper.
3
State the judge's action for the capable executor
If the executor is both trustworthy (amīn) and capable (qādir), the judge may not remove him. Instead, he must be retained. The deceased's choice is honored.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلى عاجز عن القيام بها ضمّ إليه غيره) : أي يضم القاضي إليه غيره)، ( ويبقى أمين يقدر) : أي إذا كان الوصي أميناً قادراً على التصرف لا يجوز للقاضي إخراجه ، بل يجب تبقيته.
And to one incapable of carrying it out, another is joined to him: that is, the judge joins another to him. And a trustworthy capable (executor) remains: that is, if the executor is trustworthy and capable of acting, the judge may not remove him, but must retain him.
Source pages p. 633

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where the appointed executor is physically or legally incapable of carrying out the duties. In such a case, the judge does not replace him entirely but instead appoints a co-executor to assist him. This preserves the deceased's choice while ensuring the estate is managed properly. If the executor is both trustworthy and capable, the judge cannot remove him; he must be retained because the deceased's selection is given priority. This ruling balances the deceased's intent with the need for proper administration.

Key Terms
ضم (damm): To join or appoint another person alongside the existing executor.
أمين (amin): A trustworthy person who can be relied upon to fulfill duties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the judge do if an executor is incapable of performing his duties?
A) Removes him entirely
B) Appoints a co-executor to assist
C) Leaves the estate unmanaged
D) Forfeits the will

Key Takeaway
The judge must add a co-executor to an incapable executor and never remove a capable one.

Wasiyyah — Bequest
beginner
6 min
Joint Executors Must Act Jointly Except for Burial
Ruling on independent authority of joint executors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule that joint executors must act jointly and identify the exception for shroud and burial preparation, explaining the rationale of necessity.

Core Idea
Joint executors must act together; only the shroud and burial allow independent action.

When a testator appoints two executors, Islamic law requires them to act jointly in administering the will. This ensures mutual oversight and faithful execution of the testator's wishes. However, an exception is made for the urgent purchase of the shroud and preparation of the deceased, where delay could cause harm to the deceased's dignity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The text establishes that when two executors are appointed, neither may act alone. Joint action is wajib (obligatory).
This applies to all estate matters unless an exception is specified.
2
Recognize the Exception
The only exception is for purchasing the shroud (kafan) and preparing the body (tajhiz). In these matters, an individual executor may act independently.
The exception is strictly limited to burial necessities.
3
Understand the Rationale
The exception is based on necessity: delay in burial preparation would harm the deceased (e.g., putrefaction). Urgency overrides the joint-action requirement.
This aligns with the Hanafi principle that necessity (darura) permits what is otherwise prohibited.
4
Confirm the Authority
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is relied upon (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school. It is agreed upon across madhhabs.
No intra-Hanafi or inter-madhhab disagreement exists on this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلى اثنين لا ينفردُ أحدُهما إلا بشراء كفنه، وتجهيزه
And to two people: neither of them acts alone except for the purchase of his shroud and his preparation.
Source pages p. 633

Commentary
When a person appoints two executors, they must act jointly in administering the estate. However, an exception is made for purchasing the shroud and preparing the deceased for burial. This is because delay in these matters causes harm to the deceased (putrefaction), so urgency overrides the requirement of joint action. This reflects the Islamic principle of necessity overriding general rules.

Key Terms
انفراد (infirad): Acting independently without the other executor's consent.
كفن (kafan): Shroud or burial cloth for the deceased.
تجهيز (tajhiz): Preparation of the deceased for burial, including washing, shrouding, and funeral.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which situation may one joint executor act alone?
A) Selling the deceased's house
B) Purchasing the shroud and preparing the body
C) Distributing the estate
D) Paying all debts

Key Takeaway
Joint executors must act jointly in all estate matters except for purchasing the shroud and preparing the deceased for burial, where independent action is permitted due to urgency.
